RATING: 8.9
A great intro to a promising board game franchise. Deck-building is less of a focus here than in the LOTR LCG... the trade-off being better casual play and superior thematic narratives. I like the campaign model -- leveraging experience points, trauma, cultists transferring between scenarios. Although it does remove some replayability, the different investigators, location cards, upgrade possibilities, and potential endings give it some legs.

I went ahead and ordered the entire Dunwich Legacy expansion series.

RATING: 3
I don't know how this game is getting consistent 7s and 8s from people. Other than the fact that it is continually hyped into the stratosphere, word should've gotten around by now that the hot new Arkham game is pretty thin.

Let's start with the good, because the bad is everything else:
-Nice component quality.
-Cards have interesting art and decent graphic frame design.
-Premise looks promising; you and a friend have crazy shit happen to you in the Lovecraft universe via story narratives and you move around locations finding clues and doing *stuff* to the world.
-Deckbuilder where you can spend earned points to better your deck, so there's clearly an incentive not to fail.
-Five unique characters with (probably) different play styles.

The everything else:
-A turnoff for me was the extremely poorly written rules. There are 3 separate books and each one repeats information from the others, with the main book repeating itself several times. It was an absolute boring slog to get through them and actually took me 2 days. It's nearly impossible to read this game's rule's without becoming more bored.

-Too easy. The game's difficulties don't add difficulty at all. There's no requirement for players to master the systems or become incrementally better as they step up the difficulty. It's just a measurement of arbitrary masochism, and by that I don't even mean interesting masochism: just "how likely do you want your failure rate?"

-The cheapness. Despite being mindlessly easy to blow through the scenarios getting a perfect outcome, several events in the game feel "cheap". In one instance of the game, after it had ended Roland took a permanent point of mind damage (Mental Trauma) because his personal weakness came out AFTER all clues had been investigated (majority by him, mind you) and he could literally not meet its conditions because he succeeded too well. He was permanently damaged outside of the game's actual play. Then once AGAIN when he suffered another mental trauma while reading the resolution rules. So many times, you will have insanely cheap things happen to you that you can do nothing to either prepare for nor mitigate because they happen outside of gameplay. Some of which feels unintentional and lazy. Some of which feels like "fuck whoever plays this, they'll just have to deal with it". I'd like to remind the reader, this game was trivially easy no matter how it was played. One last cheap mechanic is the red tentacle token. I've seen other reviews saying it's the bane of the fun in this game and...it IS. An auto fail isn't fun. It isn't tense. No matter how many mental gymnastics a designer or fan tries to do, auto-failure in a game about risk vs reward is just loony.

-The narrative is shit. I don't usually straight up curse out a game in my reviews but fuck this game's disgusting writing. It reads like a 6th grader whose dad told him about a Lovecraft story he read one night, then the kid tried writing his own the next day at school. Go and read these cards, they're absolutely comically bad. We laughed at our table when anything had to be read to progress. This is coming from a group that's used to both board games and RPGs; of which this is neither. It's clearly trying to be an RPG accessible to board gamers and its failing magnificently.

-The deckbuilding isn't. You basically never need to change much of anything. Sure, you can but it won't make a huge difference until endgame. The 'difficulty' does ramp up as you play but the deck only changes by a couple points worth of cards. It's a slow roll that never quite has any satisfying payoff.

-Cheap assembly. This game comes with barely enough to play, and even that amount feels underpackaged. You get a couple sheets of tokens (more on this bullshit later) and around 250 total cards. But then you split the cards into a Mythos deck, 5 player decks, and OH WE FORGOT TO MENTION: since we're being cheap here, you can't play any two characters in the core that leech from each other's color. Because screw you, we don't feel like including extra copies. The game feels spread too thin among all its cards and definitely begs you to buy one of the expansions immediately. No more is this apparent than when you realize it only has 2 scenarios to play, one of which ends in about 15 minutes. So the entire game is just replaying the same bullshit over and over...I guess at a "harder" difficulty with slightly different cards?

-The chaos bag is just a fucking die. The game, advertisements, and reviewers have all played up the chaos bag as some innovative mechanic for randomization. It's not. It's literally a die with a custom number of faces. That's neither good nor bad. It just is. While playing, we realized it would be a lot more fun if you removed the tokens from the bag when picked until all were picked. This strictly changes the game for the better as now that you know what can happen next, tensions can rise (when its bad) or you can finally stop being agitated at a run of four fucking red tentacles and -4s.

-The skill checks are the only mechanic in the entire game. Oh I know the rule book(s) are filled to the brim with mechanics but they're all fake. For every new name, number, or color they give a mechanic, it all boils down to drawing a token from the chaos bag (i.e. rolling the chaos die), then comparing your numbers with the target number. It's boring. It's the entire game; all of it. Everything you do boils down to that with absolutely nothing else. Want to investigate? Skill check. Want to attack? Skill check. Evade? Random event? Story event? ALL skill checks. All you do is gamble some skill boosts before the chaos bag (I mean die) then draw (roll) to see if you succeed. That's it. That's the entire game from the moment you sit down to the moment you go home. Everything is just a thinly reflavored version of this exact same mechanic. All your deckbuilding and narrative and whatever other stuff you think you're doing just boils down to skill checks every action.

-The tokens look like dogshit and are totally unnecessary. Fantasy Flight has the uncanny ability to overproduce any game and underproduce it simultaneously. Too few cards to feel like a functional deckbuilder. Too many goddamn tokens to make any sense. I swear this game is 1d6 SAN loss every time you look at it. Most of the tokens (clue/doom/resource) could've just been tracker cards or anything else that isn't insanely fiddly and gets in the way of play. Obviously tokens aren't a big deal but when you think "card game" you don't think "needs twice the space just so tokens don't get everywhere". This is a minor nitpick. As is the statement that they look terrible. The tokens basically resemble the uninspired, lazy design of Edritch Horror, which I'm convinced they stole from D&D Castle Ravenloft et al. board games. Those games have the exact same yawn-syndrome of single color design everywhere. It's nothing to look at. And in a board game, you want something interesting to look at.

Nitpicks aside, this game has serious flaws that hamper it from providing anyone with experience in any of what Arkham Horror LCG is trying to do from enjoying it. If you have played a deckbuilder, this will feel like it comes short. If you've roleplayed, this will make you actually cringe or laugh. If you like design, your eyes will yawn. Hard pass for me and my group. Major regret buying this.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Wow. Just.. wow. 

Perhaps not the best adventure game I've ever played, but it takes the cake for the best LCG cooperative adventure experience (I detest Lord of the Rings: LCG, as does Jen -- and she [i]adores[/i] Tolkien).

I'd rate this a 10/10 if it maintains its edge as the card pool grows. Right now it shows a lot of promise, and threatens to make good on that promise at almost every turn, but certain elements have yet to be fully realized. 

For example: It is really cool the players can get XP to spend on card upgrades between quests. However, for any particular class, those upgrades are currently rather paltry, and I was throwing XP away after the second scenario since I was happy with my deck.  

8/10 (for now).

:arrowE: [u]Synopsis[/u]:
Build a deck of cards to represent the abilities, equipment, and flavor of your investigator, then face other decks of horrid effects, discover clues, and advance plot acts against the racing clock of DOOM AGENDAS.

:arrowE: [u]What I like most[/u]:
THIS is the RPG/board game cross-over I've been looking for for a while. The co-op nature of the LCG means I don't have to scramble to own all of it all the time, and the deck-as-investigator's-character-sheet is awesome. RNG is handled through a bag of variable tokens, replacing custom dice with a fascinating blend of risk aversion and buffed card effects against failure. The game manages to evoke a powerful story-telling aspect while giving players lots of crunchy options to play WELL in addition to immersing themselves in rich, potent narrative. I've barely scratched the surface, but I'm smitten.

:arrowE: [u]What I like least[/u]:
Remember that time when you had to discard half of your hand and then got attacked by monsters without any chance to set up your weapons, spells, or abilities? Yeah. Me too.

[hr]
11/16/17 EDIT: I'm bumping this from 8 to 10 on the basis that it really is such a riveting experience, but also such a STRONG GAME. It really nails the theme and mechanics of an unfolding mystery which manages to evoke great emotion and drama (and hilarity!) while also rewarding thoughtful deck construction and player decision.

RATING: 9
Viciante al máximo. Para ser un juego de cartas transmite de una manera genial la sensación de ir resolviendo un misterio y explorando nuevas localizaciones.

Los mapas tienen variantes generadas al azar para que no sean siempre lo mismo. Aun así los desafíos no dependen tanto de conocer el mapa o la forma optima de resolver el misterio ya que hay encuentros al azar, cambios en el mapa y la constante de que tu personaje (o baraja) no es siempre la misma. Además, la campaña tiene variantes en función de tus acciones en las misiones anteriores con lo que siempre hay posibilidad de hacer las cosas de otra manera para ver que sucede y/o sacarte finales diferentes.

Y hablo de decisiones en plan "Haces caso al consejo del misterioso personaje y quemas tu casa... o no confías en el y la dejas como está?"

Es un juego al que siempre tengo ganas de jugar. A dos jugadores va genial y andas ahí estrujándote el cerebro todo el rato para ver como os podéis ayudar el uno al otro o como conseguir determinados objetivos de la manera más rápida y eficaz.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Wow. This game is amazing.
It is a crossbreed among a complex card game and an RPG. The idea is wonderful, and the execution very compelling.
It is obviously a derivative of LOTR LCG (it has the same designers!), but it is almost completely a different game and experience.
There is a trade off between the possibility to build large comboish tableaus (LOTR has it, while AH limits player much more) and the campaign system, with the sense of progression and engagement of this quasi-RPG.
The mechanics are sound and polished, and I confess I love the chaos bag.
Bravo, FFG. Another winner LCG.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
This game runs the gamut. Some aspects are good, some aspects are middling and other aspects are terrible. Due to that, the comments here will be quite extensive. My rating is a combination of the solo experience and the cooperative experience where both use one core set and the expansion cards are only unlocked in order of use (e.g. when first playing the core set, you cannot use the Dunwich cards). If I ignored my enjoyment of this game as a solo experience, this would likely get a 4 or lower from me.

First, let's discuss what's good. 

The Lovecraftian story is what shines here. The acts, agenda and scenario text create a good story. Additional story elements emerge based on the investigator decks and the encounter decks. The fact that you don't see the entirety of either of those decks contributes to the emergent story.  Also contributing are the various card types, the combinations allowed in the various investigator decks, and the variation of the card effects themselves. Plus, on average, the artwork does a good job of bringing the theme through. The campaign and permanent health/horror effects also contribute to the story telling of this game. This game does well as a solo game where you slowly soak in the story and don't need to worry about making sure everyone is enjoying the game.

The theory of this game providing interesting options for deck construction is a positive, but the game is not there yet, especially if you are without multiple core sets.

Now, for the middling.  

The game play is shallow. The card play and skill checks are two aspects that are done better in other games. Every skill check-generating action feels the same (e.g., investigating feels the same as attacking, etc.) and that makes for a pretty procedural, unexciting experience (ignoring the story). For most cards, you choose whether you use a card for its text effect or its skill check modifier. CDGs do a better job of making this type of decision more compelling, due to the choice in CDGs feeling more strategically flexible and player-driven. Here, the decision to burn a card during a skill check feels less about your cleverness in deciding what will help you progress, and more a simple weighing between the importance of the skill check and card's effect. This is also where drawing only a portion of your deck and its resulting chaos has a negative effect, as the card draw is more chaos than consistent. 

Additionally, as a comparison, the card play in Gloomhaven is leaps and bounds more fun than the card play here (in both the decision of what cards to play and how it injects randomness). Looking to a different game, Nemo's War does a better job of making skill check wagers feel meaningful but not wasteful if the outcome is a failure. Now, all this can be hand-waved away as being "thematic" of the doom-rich Mythos universe, but that seems merely an excuse for the lackluster mechanisms employed in this game. This game attempts to provide both chaos and control, but mixes them poorly. Other games (e.g., Gloomhaven and Nemo's War, among others) do control better. Mansions of Madness, 2nd Edition, does chaos better simply because it embraces the chaos more fully and doesn't obfuscate the chaos with comparatively uninteresting control mechanisms. 

This game is essentially simple, but the rule books add unneeded complexity. Normally, I like FFGs rule book structure, but its out of place or poorly done here. The rule book remains by your side for much longer than it should be. Most of the undue rule book-generated complexity could have been cured through additional player aids and keyword references.  

Finally, due to the structure of the core box, the deck construction aspects of this game are greatly reduced in importance until you get a number of expansions (or get multiple core sets, which I refuse to do, since you double the cost to receive the small portion of the core box you actually need). There is some replayability in this game due to the variation in investigators, ability to construct different decks, and each scenario only using a subset of the possible cards. But the experience of going through a scenario for the first time can never be recaptured and that's the biggest strength of this game.

Finally, for the awful. 

The core set of this game makes the co-op experience about as boring as it could be. Here, you often end up with investigators that are silo-ed to certain actions. The interaction between the players is relegated to table talk and donating cards to skill checks. The Warhammer ACG core set felt more cooperative/interactive than this core set. Honestly, at the current state of the game, having one core set makes this game almost unplayable at 2 players, due to the low level of interactivity and the reduced number of investigator combinations that can be played. The Dunwich and Carcosa expansions improve on the interactivity between players via the introduction of new cards. Unless you have a group that is fully invested in the story telling of this game, I would not recommend the cooperative experience of this game. 

The production choices of this game and some of its expansions are pretty terrible for what you pay. As touched on above, the core set is playable at 1 player but barely playable at 2. You get zero ability to organize the game so that set up time is simplified (no Dominion-style box here). The stand-alone expansions (e.g., Rougarou) are vastly inferior in both card quality and instruction quality. It feels like they've provided the players with the absolute minimum of what's required to play this game. It borders on shameful.

Arkham Horror: The Card Game – Carnevale of Horrors (2016)
Arkham Horror: The Card Game – Curse of the Rougarou (2016)
Arkham Horror: The Card Game – The Dunwich Legacy (2017) full cycle
Arkham Horror: The Card Game – The Path to Carcosa (2017)
Unseen Horrors Playmat

RATING: N/A
I have the core set, Dunwich Horror and the first two scenarios of the Dunwich campaign at this moment. So far it is excellent and challenging. Compared to another game I enjoy, LotR LCG, there is much less focus on deck building for a scenario and more on starting with a basic deck and enhancing it by gaining XP in scenarios and spending it to upgrade cards. Fun, thematic and a great LCG so far!  Currently pre-ordering the packs as soon as they appear and have no reason to stop yet!

RATING: 8.6
Living Card game with a story. Probably best played with 1 or 2 players.

Rules are difficult at first, but later on well explained with a Reference manual going deeper into the system. Less groundbreaking as the LOTR LCG but it has a bit better solo play. Replayability is not its strong point as much attention was given to follow a close story line. But you always play with other investigators and after X time you forget the exact stories andbor locations that are randomised anyway.

The advantage of these solo LCG's is that you can come back to them long after the "hype" died off and even within 20 years you can play them on your own.

I dropped the initial score somewhat after a break of almost one year and writing a new review on the BGG forum. It is always good to return to a game with a new look after one year. The game is still great.

EH is still the best in this genre as it has wide open boards and strategic planning. Mansions of Madness2 is the best story teller but this AH LCG has better replayability than MoM2.

RATING: 9
Lovecrafts Cthulu Mythos is being placed on many games these days, however, I am a fan of this world and I think it fits perfectly for a cooperative game. 
This game is a cooperative deck builder with relatively simply mechanics and a clear turn structure. The deck building is a big part of this game and it really pays off if you try to give players specific roles. One has to be the muscle that helps you get rid of strong enemies, another one has to be good in finding clues that are necessary to progress in the story. If you play with more than two players (which we do) there is room for more archetypes. 
Although there is a somewhat limited number of card types scenarios typically feel different enough. The investigators also play quite differently, even if they are the same color. The card pool becomes bigger with each scenario which makes deck building and the development of archetype more interesting as time goes by.
What I like about many campaign based games, and this in particular is the fact that decisions will matter later in the campaign even if that means some snowballing. Usually the better you do early, the easier it is later. 
The growing card pool and the adjustable difficulty gives the game also some replay value. 
One of the minor negative aspects is the small number of new cards for each color in the scenario packs. It is likely that you won´t include cards from every expansion in your deck.

If you like cooperative play, the Cthulu mythos and deck building this one is for you.

RATING: 9
This game has me jonesing for more content.  Each scenario feels unique in both mechanisms and narrative. After the first deluxe expansion, the deck building possibilities are already interesting for most of the investigators. Play scales well for different player counts. 

Not the easiest teach. I think teaching the game during play works best.  A far amount of iconography and terminology to pick up.  There is no shortage of moving parts to keep track of during each phase. You will making rules blunders, but you will learn from them.

The biggest negative is overall cost.  After buying one or more core sets you will pay $15 per month for mythos packs and $30 every 7th month for a new deluxe. Not to mention component upgrades, storage, and sleeving to protect your investment.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I was excited to try this game as a huge fan of the LotR LCG. I would say that I had mixed impressions overall. There are some significant changes here from LotR LCG, and while some I think work well, others are a disappointment for me. First, I really enjoy the new concept of locations. Investigators move around and attract new monsters and location abilities. This introduces the idea of splitting up or staying together and I think that is quite smart. I also think they have done a nice job is simplifying the way resources work. Instead or individual resources or the four spheres, there is now just a generic resource that can be spent. I also thought the limited campaign style elements they introduced are fun and done well. There isn’t a ton of player progression and growth, but it does a better job of tying the three base set scenarios together than LotR LCG did. Finally, the way that they simplified the doom tracking is simpler and easier to manage overall. 

However, there are also other changes that I didn’t care for much at all. The exciting and puzzly combat system of LotR LCG is now gone. If an active monster in engaged with you, it just hits you for a fixed amount of damage. No blocking to speak of etc. This felt bland and a letdown when comparing to the LotR system. I also didn’t really enjoy the chaos bag (well chaos (please provide your own) bag). While I can really appreciate how this is a very elegant way to tailor the difficulty of a scenario, it also feels very random. I didn’t hate it because of the options for push your luck mitigation, but it just felt more random when compared to the shadow cards in Lotr LCG. I also didn’t really enjoy the fact that you either have to evade or fight a monster engaged with you or you will take damage. It felt like one random monster draw from the deck could dictate your entire turn, and I was missing some sense of choice when this happened (i.e. play a card first, etc.). I also missed the ability to control multiple characters from LotR LCG. The puzzle of how to commit your characters from this game is really excellent and here it is missing. Lastly, as a completely personal preference I much prefer the LotR setting over the Cthulu one.

In the end I think FFG has delivered a solid co-op LCG that will get better with time. They have streamlined some things from LotR, but in doing so they have eliminated some of the best aspects (combat, resource management, control of multiple characters for more choice) in my eyes. The constant skill checks and random modifiers, while fitting perfectly in the Arkham/Eldritch line that FFG has created, just take me a bit out of the game and make things feel more random. I prefer LotR overall as a game and I think it provides more of a challenge and mental workout. However, if you are interested in getting in on the ground floor of a co-op LCG and are intimidated by the large number of expansions out for LotR LCG, then this is a fine place to start I think. Instead of an improved and perfected LotR LCG experience, I think what you have here is a streamlined (for better and worse) version that will appeal to some fans, but probably leave LotR LCG fans feeling a bit underwhelmed.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8

This game is a great investment, I'd highly recommend it. It's just good to keep in mind that this isn't exactly a finished experience, just yet. Once Fantasy Flights has delivered on the promise of its future expansions, and delivered more campaigns to play through, this might be the closest thing to a perfect cooperative gaming experience I've ever had.

RATING: 10
I rate Eldritch Horror quite highly (you can read my review below). This game is everything I like about Eldritch Horror, but better. It's faster-paced, richer in its storytelling, more challenging, and more strategic. It's a truly exceptional game.

To date, there have been 13 officially released scenarios (but there are only three in the base game.) That's an excellent amount of replayability, which is further enhanced by the randomness of card drawing and a wide range of investigators to choose from. Like Eldritch Horror, the unique theme of each investigator really shines forth. Each and every one of them feels greatly different to play and tells a compelling story. You'll want to try them all. 

There's Roland, a strong and smart FBI agent who would like to keep all the occult happenings hushed up; Daisy, a physically limited but fearlessly inquisitive librarian; Skids, an ex-convict who excels at giving monsters the slip; Agnes, a waitress with frightening mystical powers. Lastly there's Wendy, a weak and lonely orphan, but for all that no less resolved to fight evil.

The investigators are quite unbalanced (and the player cards even moreso) but this is not vitally important in a co-op game. 

Another strength is that the game scales very well between 1 and 4 players. Solo play is a fun challenge--it's very fast-paced (a scenario might take 30 minutes or less), and it's kind of fun to imagine one person trying to single-handedly take on the horrors of the cosmos. 2 and 3 work very well, and greatly increase the strategy possibilities. At 4, you start to get a little bit of drag, but it's definitely playable. I'd budget about thirty minutes per person per scenario, plus perhaps ten minutes for setup.

A few mechanics deserve notice as particularly brilliant. The chaos bag is simply more fun than using dice. Even more important, it makes the game's difficulty very easy to scale. Simply swap a few tokens and you've got a dramatically more or less challenging game. The campaign system is very well done. The trauma mechanic is just right; defeat is punished, but not excessively harshly. Spending XP, of course, is quite fun. Right now XP options are a bit limited, but the card pool grows every month. The decision to keep player decks quite small (around 33 cards) is good--it allows for consistency and strategy in deck design with only 2 copies of each card. I love the weakness system. Weaknesses add a bit of tension to every card draw, and really bring out the theme of each investigator. 

One downside is that the core set only includes one copy of most player cards. You'll need to buy a second one, therefore, to do any real deckbuilding. Even then, if you want to run a game with 2-4 players, you might wish you had 4 or even more core sets. 

The campaign in the core set (Night of the Zealot) is pretty short and simple. It's fun, and I saw enough in it to play it through five or six times. I suspect it will not be seen, in the long run, as the best this game has to offer. Its second scenario, Midnight Masks, nonetheless deserves mention as a clear standout.

RATING: 7
Just interviewing people about their experiences with this game, it seems like the key is to buy two copies of the base set so you can get access to two of key cards that they give you only one of. This is crazy; it annoys the hell out of me the way FFG ships core sets that don't really give you what you need to actually enjoy the game. Anyway, I've gotten a few of the expansions so I'm looking forward to giving this another go with a few more cards, but I haven't steeled myself to buying another core set yet.
---
Finished the scenario arc in the main box. It's an odd game. The mechanics are there - it's a solid set of systems, much more so than the board game or even the Star Wars and Lord of the Rings LCGs. The narrative elements are clever and satisfying. But the balances, as usual, seem weirdly out of whack. You draw through a minimal number of cards from your deck in a game (maybe you see 10 of 35-ish?) which makes it super-chaotic. Sometimes, like in our last game, you draw one of the impossible-to-defeat boss monsters out of the deck on turn 1 and the game becomes basically unwinnable right there. Sometimes you get one of the handful of vital and powerful cards from your deck in the deal, sometimes you don't. You spend 6 or 10 XP getting better cards into your deck and then maybe there is a 50/50 or chance you'll see one of them the next game. The quality of the experience is weirdly dependent on how well/poorly/interestingly you draw, and there just isn't a lot you can do about it and there is a huge amount of variation in the card flow.

I'll be curious to see where they go with this - introducing more cards to the deckbuilding to even out the chaos would obviously be helpful. I might be good for the upcoming deluxe expansion, just to see where it goes, but I doubt it's something I'll get sucked in to. We'll see.
---
Other than Netrunner,  haven't liked any of FFG's [C|L]CGs very much - they're mostly pretty procedural and mechanical and don't really engage with their theme much; both Star Wars and Lord of the Rings are pretty abstract with a bit of fan service. But this was OK. The game design is reasonably streamlined, it has real nods to Lovecraftian stories (albeit very pulpy ones), and the way the three scenarios are tied together is clever. Star Wars, Lord of the Rings, and the previous incarnations of Cthulhu LCG all felt very mechanical and derivative to me; this one actually felt like someone designed it with the goal of making it at least somewhat Lovecraftian.

As a game, the resource management and tactical choices you make are still not super-compelling, and the biggest problem is probably just that you don't cycle enough cards. With 35-ish cards in your deck and cycling only maybe as few as 10 to at most 20 a game, it's a bit of a crapshoot whether you get what you need in a timely manner. Some of the Weakness cards are really brutal (the cop's Coverup in particular is almost game-breaking) and the strategy with these seems to be to just not draw them, and certainly not to draw them early. Seriously, don't play the cop. Starting with two mental traumas going into scenario 3 is a back breaker.

Anyway, I enjoyed this, it's not the total package, but to me it's way more fun than Arkham Horror or Elder Sign and it's a nice 2p. But it's expensive for what it is; $40 MSRP for a 4 hour campaign for two players is very, very pricey by boardgame standards. You're buying into the whole LCG if you're buying this; it's the only way you'll eventually get value. We'll see how it unfolds, if it pulls me back more than I expect. I might revisit it in 6 months when the environment is more mature.

RATING: 6
Based on 1 play with 4 players (first scenario):

Hooray! A Lovecraftian game I don't actively despise! This is a weird one to comment on after just one scenario, since you're likely using prebuilt decks (we did). Like its boarded counterparts, there isn't a lot of strategy in the game itself. But that's what makes it feel ripe for the conversion to a deck-based game. The game is random as hell, but giving you the ability to modify the random element by building your deck seems like a great addition to the formula.

Lots of quality of life improvements since the original FFG version of Arkham Horror also speed the game up considerably. Our first scenario took about 2.5 hours and that was with a group who hadn't even looked over their decks beforehand.

I don't think I'll ever consider this a great game, but it's a fun enough thing to do with a group. Much better than TIME Stories.

RATING: 6
Played through the base campaign on standard (so 3 plays so far). Enjoyed the experience over-all but disappointed vs the glowing praise given by Shut Up & Sit Down. Pain in the butt to set up, especially considering there are only 2 of us playing. You do a little bit of deck-building but may only see 1/3 of your deck during a given game, which can be disappointing. 

Even after playing through the campaign, there are enough rules and exceptions that we were still constantly looking stuff up and made mistakes (For example, a card that says Test Skill vs 3, for each point you fail by take damage; we thought if you drew a -5 and failed by 5 you would take 5 damage, but actually there is another rule somewhere that you can only go to 0 so would take only 3 damage). This can slow down play a lot until you are confident about things, and mistakes can wildly swing the difficulty of a given scenario.

Each scenario took us about 2 to 2.5 hours to play through. For a two player cooperative game this is almost excruciatingly long, though like I said, we spent quite a bit of time rule checking. Essentially every turn we were consulting the rule book or googling forums.

I liked it more than my partner. If we had a full grasp on the game I can see how the replay value comes from trying different strategies and character archetypes. But even so because each game took us so long it's not jumping on to the table after work like some of our other two player games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
(Own 2 copies of the base game, plus several of the pre-painted minis to sub in for the cardboard investigator chits: IMO this means I have enough cards to provide decks for 2 players, 1 Core and .5 expansions containing player cards per player seems necessary so far; I also have 1 copy of every expansion currently released and will be keeping up with this until further notice via subscription).

Overall I find that this is an interesting take on Arkham that in many ways is an improvement on the board game for me, but with some caveats.

First, the plus is that this game much more effectively communicates a story to me than the board game does. Obviously with story driven campaign cards and flavour throughout the campaign this was a given, but it really emphasized to me for the first time how much more abstractly thematic (for lack of a better concept, it's weird but it conveys meaning) AH is in comparison to this. The player decks and campaign ooze theme, which I really like. Second, the customizing, though limited without additional purchases, does give players a lot more leeway to build their team to fit their own preferences, this is pretty appealing as well.

At first blush I'm not sold on the way monsters move in this game relative to the board game, but I think expansions will likely fix this. The complexity of monster movement is one of the annoyances of the base game, but it is also one of the ways that it ratchets up the tension, there isn't as much of that here as monsters tend to hang out where they spawn and there isn't a huge inherent penalty for allowing them to build up as there is in AH.

The major downside I can see for some (not for me personally) is that replay seems modest. I play games like this to experience the story, after you've done that the charm is greatly reduced. That makes the core set box a pretty short value, as it takes 1.5ish hrs. per story, for a total of 5 hours max for each run through the campaign. I have no problem paying the price for ongoing changes, but this one isn't going to be cheap to keep up with. The base set mini-campaign at least does seem to have a severe difficulty ramp for part 3, which I assume is intended to make people play it at least a couple of times to experience different twists, but I doubt I would be interested in going through any story arc very often as the scenarios included here, while good for tutorial purposes, are really not super strong for repeat play for me. Different investigators / pairings do help a bit, but that is only really facilitated fully by having 2 Cores or a lot of expansion material. 

It is possible to get some replay by cranking up the difficulty, but for me, personally, there is no way I could see myself playing any higher than Standard without more powerful options from the expansions as the game can already give you some nasty swings on Easy or Standard. Having additional cards to mitigate the randomness of your own deck (by adding doubles of good 0 level cards, by removing less useful stuff) does a lot to make the higher difficulties manageable, but even with a crazy deck the big negative chits would make the game very random / dependent on great chit drawing and all the higher difficulties really do is greatly increase the chances of being screwed by drawing a huge negative modifier.

Playing off the above one niggle: there really aren't any options for deckbuilding with only 1 box. I personally don't care to optimize things to within an inch of their life, but it would have been nice to have at least a few extra cards for each class sprinkled in so that there are a few options at the start of the game. As it is, I bought a second copy because it is required to allow any combination of characters, and I didn't want to limit our options to only a couple of different combinations. That had the side effect of giving us some ability to tailor the decks. Make no mistake though, this is not a deck-building game per se, as you basically put together a starting deck and then just tweak it lightly throughout the campaign. A second base set is very nice though, as it gives you the ability to specify roles a lot more and to eliminate cards that are not as strong for given characters. The overall approach to deckbuilding in this one is appealing to me, as I wanted something where I could help my gf build a base deck and then let her tweak it; and contrasts with games like LotR where you can rebuild your deck each game. Great for CCG/LCG heads, but not so much for normal, more casual gamers. In the post-Dunwich era you can probably get away with only 1 Core if you want to buy a series of expansions, I still like having duplicates of the Core stuff as there are many staple cards that it would be annoying not to have duplicates of during the deckbuilding phase.

Basically though, I like a lot of what is being done here. The chaos bag is a nice take on randomness and with some tweaks to the types of monster movement and the like I think this will morph into a long term winner. Shame the first box was so short on content though, I already want more!

It is also worth noting that this is a big money sink. To get any sort of satisfaction with 2 players I felt obligated to buy 2 Cores, and to make sure that we are not playing with too much overlap from there on out. I would not even think of playing with 3-4 players unless one of the those players had an additional collection of Cores + expansions to make their (and their partner, if 4) deck with as otherwise you really aren't able to make the decks as effective. This is not a big problem for me, as I only play this with my gf, but if you want to supply a group of 4 and want to really experience the deck building you need to sink some serious coin into this game. Honestly, if you want to play with more than 2 people, then let them try it out with some intro decks using your Core set(s) and then force them to get their own stuff. Supplying an entire group a real free-form pool of cards is not the intent of these types of games and would be a huge money sink and is not something that someone should do unless they (A) have a huge amount of extra gaming money to waste and (B) have friends who are dirt poor and can't cover the cost of their own stuff.

Aside: the game seems perfectly manageable with 2 investigators / players. I think it could end up being pretty hairy with only 1, even on an easier difficulty as there are so many ways to end up in auto-damage situations or to need the use of skills that are not available to your character.

Final note: the trickle of releases doesn't bother me from a budgetary standpoint, but it has slowed our play of this game down a bit. I really am starting to be of the mind that it is best to just have a complete cycle and then play it through at that point. This is mainly because it's a bit of a chore to keep track of decklists, or more basically, what the heck is going on in the story when you have to wait for time periods between plays. I play this game for theme, and it breaks the mood to have big gaps between plays. I started Dunwich, but then put it on pause for about 6 months as I didn't want to just play 1 adventure per month. Unless you are into very sporadic play for this, it seems like the best way to go.

RATING: 8
Villámbeszámoló:
Viszonylag egyszerű játék, de nagyon komplex a sok kis apróság miatt amire folyamatosan figyelni kell - nem a nyugis játékok közé fog tartozni ilyen szempontból sem. Megtanulni nem volt nehéz, de nagyon sok módosító, változó fog jelentkezni, és mindenre figyelni kell. Az első, tanuló játék egyébként kb. 2 óra volt, el is buktuk a küldit. A második kb. 40 perc alatt lement, igaz, az előkészületekkel itt már nem kellett pepecselni.
Rendkívül hangulatos dologról van szó, ami részben a MoM, részben a Gyűrűk ura LCG utánérzetet adja, de azt remekül.

Két fontos tudnivaló:
1) A kampánymód remek, de rövid. Két délután alatt végigjátszható és máris kívánja az ember az új kieget...
2) ...hacsak nem fut neki újra, módosított paklival, és próbál meg még jobb eredményt elérni. Ekkor viszont már nincs, vagy minimalizálódik a meglepetés ereje, így kicsit átmegy amolyan Hanabi féle - ki tud jobb kombóval győzedelmeskedni dologba. Nem baj ez, de a kezdeti meglepetésfaktor csökkenésével nehogy a játék élvezeti értéke is romoljon.

Meglepetésként ért:
A játék egyenesen utasít rá, hogy csak hangulati elemekkel kommunikáljunk. És ettől tényleg szuperebb lesz a játék, jár a keksz. De egy idő után (pláne amikor a maximális kombóval való győzelem a cél) ez ígyis-úgyis átmegy abba, hogy csak a kártyák nevét vagy a tesztmódosító számát mondjuk be.

Nagy pluszpont jár a húzózsákos dologért: nincs kocka, van más randomizáció.

A grafika 10/10, a hangulat 10/10. Az előkészület, a várhatóan számtalan kieget hozó pénzszívás miatt csak LCG kedvelőknek ajánlom, de nekik nagyon. Na jó, picit azoknak is, akik élnek-halnak az FFG-s lovecrafti világért. :)

Amire még kíváncsi vagyok: milyen 1, 3 és 4 fővel.

RATING: 6
Why I got rid of it:  

The game was fun but it was a beast to set up.  It had so many special key words and rules, like most games from fantasy flight.  Each pack of cards required 20 min of trying to learn the special stuff for that deck.  It was hard to organize the cards and keep track of all of them.  

Finally, the game was too hard.  It had a fun mechanism to get experience and build up your character but we rarely lived that long and could not get attached to our characters as they kept dying.

I would gladly play someone elses copy.  Then they have to buy it, organize it and listen to the whines of me and other players dying and not keeping the upgrade we worked so hard to get.

RATING: 9
Fantastic! Best RPG experience in a board game. Your deck is your character, and the fact that you can't massively rework it each scenario (a la Lord of the Rings) means you get to know it really well, flaws and all. You gain experience which can be spent to tweak it and buy better cards, but the small amount you get each adventure means the deck changes gradually. And you can be forced to add bad cards to it due to scenarios. Brilliant. 

The bag drawing mechanism is also very clever. Unlike dice, the odds can be changed each scenario by adding new chits, which can very depending upon the results of a game. But what makes the game is the fact that you play a scenario and, win or lose, advance to the next one. That makes each game feel as if it has consequences...cards can be added or not to your decks and chits to the bag depending upon your actions, and choices can come back to haunt or help you later in the campaign. 

The biggest criticism is the cost of each adventure. You really need to play each one multiple times to make it worth it, but once you  know the secrets you are less likely to want to do so,  FFG has tried to make sure the replays will be different by including variations of some cards that you randomize when you play, so the game may go differently next time. Good idea. 

I hope the quality of the Dunwich Legacy campaign is indicative of those in the future. Right now this is easily the best of the Lovecraft games on the market.

RATING: 6
Pros:
Fun chit bag pulling system instead of the usual dice roll to pass checks. Adjustable difficulty levels. Hand management and deck building options grow with more expansions. Story content is cool.

Cons:
Campaign system is well done but long and requires dedicated group. Game requires multiple decks for up to 4 players which increases cost.

4 player experience feels like each player has to specialize their deck to be successful which limits how many viable choices for actions there are. For a cooperative game, I think I look more for an adventure than an optimization exercise. Maybe with fewer and/or different player styles, the other story driven mechanics would take over more. The token bag mechanic is awesome.

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Bought this very excited a couple of months ago, after having a really positive experience with another FFG game, Eldritch Horror, which my two sons and I greatly enjoy. Have been an unironic Cthulhu mythos fan and player of the Chaosium pen and paper RPG since the 80's. Plus bought hard into all the positive hype about AH: TCG. So I was primed and really, really wanted to like it. Which makes my disappointment all the more crushing. 

The beautiful and varied cards, the well-written scenarios and flavor text, the typically FFG high quality chits and counters -- none of this changes the fact that the gameplay is super clunky, not smooth, forcing pauses every couple of minutes for in-depth rule searches through three different books (Quick Start Guide, Scenario Guide, Reference Guide) with lots of confusing duplicate information, and mostly gets in the way of enjoying the story and situations. I have tried to play this twice already, and the enjoyment fuse has never been lit. I never get to the point where the mechanics recede into the background and I just have a good time. 

Also, personal peeve, I just don't like the token mechanic to modify skill checks. What's wrong with tried-and-true dice, which Eldritch Horror does really well, leading to tense moments of suspense (and hopefully triumph) when the dice go your way? And if you're going to make me use tokens instead of dice, FFG, then at least include a nice thematic bag to enhance the experience. 

My Bottom Line: An ambitious effort to replicate a story-driven RPG, using cards instead of a Game Master and character sheets, spoiled by overly clunky game mechanics and poorly designed rule books. I'm giving this to my older son, perhaps he will have a better time making it work than I have.

RATING: 9
After playing 3 times with core game scenario,
failed each single time to resolve scenario that we want (i play it with my partner). It is horror game (what do you expect eh?)

It is fun game for 2 player, even though the limited character combination with core game, it's co-op shining through. ALWAYS STAY TOGETHER and FIND THAT CLUE DAMN IT!.

The mechanic for each character are unique, seriously unique man!. It's well develop by FFG. I raise my cup of tea for the designer. Well done. They develop it with class character (sound RPG right?), each class have stats (yeah it's RPG pre-made character) like Willpower, Lore, Attack and Agility. The stats are used for test skill from encounter card. 

This game was claim by the designer are story driven and RPG-esque. Well they are right. The story advance by resource called dooms and clues token. The doom token always fuelled the Agenda Card, and the investigator (player) must race with doom token to collect clues for advancing the Act Card. 

Sound easy right, NO! THIS IS HORROR GAME!. The designer make state of Analysis Paralysis
for player in good balance between doing 2 action from 9 option of action (AP already), and you still dealing with monsters, encounters, and your own cards to make finding clues less important (that's just wrong, FIND THAT CLUES DAMN IT!). well it's fun when you ignoring the monsters and just digging your cards for preferable asset card and suddenly everything gone wrong in next phase. TOLD YOU SO! FIND THAT CLUES!

It is fun game that came from horror game. Never expect that horror game can be this hard and fun to play.

The design are well develop, great illustration, nice design for each decks card, the Art-Nouveau style was gorgeously implemented in this game with time era of Lovecraftian. 

The downside of this game are the cycle pack. when you buy the expansion, you got the new character, new deck of cards, and new story to begin, BUT the big box expansion does not contain big portion of the plot story. You must buy the rest of cycle if you begin the expansion with almost 6-7 cycle pack, just to end a story. Story that maybe you enjoy for hours to play with.

But even with core game you still get various story, from player who home-brew their own cycle. This generous people are the best. There's almost 12-13 story that ready to Print and Play.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Arkham Horror LCG seems to be all of the rage. I told myself I would never get into another LCG, but decided to give this one a shot anyway. I decided to do this because:

a) It's very story driven your first few times through a particular scenario/campaign.

b) You can still enjoy it as a coop when you know the story, by trying various kinds of decks.

c) You only needed two Core Sets to have a play set of cards versus the usual three.*

d) Most scenarios are linked in a campaign where you will upgrade your character between scenarios and gain new weaknesses from the horrors you could potentially encounter.

After having played it, I'm disappointed. First, I don't like that cards you spend your experience on to upgrade your character are more powerful versions of cards you already have. I was hoping you could add new and unique cards. This could be detrimental if you build a bad deck. If you were swapping in completely new cards, you could be changing the focus of your deck after seeing what did/didn't work. I realize you could just replay the scenario and make a new deck, but it's just not what I wanted. It's my own fault for not looking into it before buying in, but I was disappointed that despite having a play set of cards with 2 Core Sets, you could potentially want more to have additional copies of neutral cards, if your collection is to be used for more than one person. That may be nitpicky, but that's what LCG's do to me.

I could never be very objective with any LCG. I say this because I really dislike having to spend time outside of gaming to make decks. This doesn't change that. I'm sure it's a good game and probably better than LotR LCG, but it's definitely not for me. Pray that I don't make the mistake of trying anymore LCG's.

RATING: 10
As a preliminary comment, I like what they’ve done here to my LOTR LCG though I’m not yet certain if I love it. I need more expansions and time to tell. 
…
I’m rewriting this comment from the future past after having played Dunwich, Rougarou, and Carnevale and now looking back on the core game with some perspective to say I … love … this game. It achieves what it sets out to do by being a complex card game with a quasi-RPG aspect. It hits on both levels for me.

I can’t talk about the game without comparing it to its sister game, LOTR LCG. There’s a tradeoff to be accepted here between the campaign of AH and the bigger play space of LOTR. That is, AH offers the ability to keep your deck between adventures and play scenarios linked as part of a larger story (which LOTR largely does not offer). Conversely, LOTR offers me the ability to build a much larger tableau due to AH’s limitation on slots. 

This prior experience colored my view of AH LCG in that AH LCG felt smaller. At first, I missed being able to build an equivalent dwarf or eagle deck and have this huge tableau of cards whose effects I trigger during my turn. In AH, you’re limited to how many allies (one!) and items you can have in play at a time (a few). That means a lot of your decisions are about which cards to replace, not add to your tableau. I didn’t like this at first and felt unsure of the game. Now, I can see the bigger picture. 

I’m glad that this is not simply a reskinned LOTR LCG but one that has its own construct. The card play is just as sophisticated and interesting as that one albeit in a different way. With a few expansion packs now, the character card choices have opened up in a way not present with just the core game. Consider that if you’re thinking about this game. The token bag works very well for me as the randomness element. Decisions you make during the campaign will add tokens to that bag which you can’t do with dice. The locations have effects or actions (ex, heal 2) that act as ersatz allies and provide another dimension not present in LOTR LCG.

Now that I have a campaign to play, I appreciate trying to make a deck that works from adventure to adventure with a few tweaks in between. Some tweaks are to add more powerful cards and some are to swap in/out equivalent cards to better focus my deck for this particular adventure. But through it all, I become attached to my characters (I like to solo with two) and get immersed in the story.

Because there is a story here. While LOTR LCG renders theme with riding down the Anduin or crawling through Khazad-dum, AH renders a true story where decisions affect the course. My investigators have choices to make that have consequences and persistent effects. The O’Bannon Gang has a bone to pick with us and one of my dudes is cursed, for example. The flavor text of the scenario book as well as the cards do a great job conveying the story that’s unfolding. 

All in all, it’s a great intersection of an RPG and a complex card game. I’m particularly enamored with the Lovecraftian genre (even though it’s filtered through FFG, but that’s another story), so it gets extra points from me in that regard.

RATING: 5
As is usual in the case of a brand new LCG there is very little customisation/deckbuilding initially, as there are simply too few cards to pick and mix the ones you want..... still we shuffled a couple of colours together and gave it a first play through.......

and it was alright, I suppose... 

Its very story driven, the flavour text on the cards matters...however this, first play especially, felt very clunky and stop start- this could be due to the newness, but honestly I was pretty bored with the rather generic story... which turned out to be big boss trapped you ...kill big boss .... and there are only the three scenarios in the box.

The map mechanic is very clever however honestly our table still was not big enough, after all the assets you put into play around your character and the map and the monsters and the token pile and the doom track and the event draw pile and the associated ....and ....and.... 

The token mechanic whereby there are hidden modifiers to your skill checks drawn randomly is interesting however it felt very much like my deck was struggling to even reach an even keel with the challenges let alone beat the skill level by enough that the token pull didn't cripple the character. interesting but not particularly well done.....again this will lessen the more you customised the deck which is simple a case of waiting for more cards to be printed.... 

hmmm not sure about this one at all :shake:

RATING: 8.5
Edited 05/18/2017: The game feels very limited without expansions, so, until I get The Dunwich Legacy cycle, it goes down from 9 to 8.5.
---

Rating after 8 plays (just the 1st scenario). I'm totally loving this game. I think that after trying the Dunwich Legacy cycle, this could easily become a 10 or at least a 9 for me.

I feel the deck building aspect very streamlined and from a narrative point of view it makes perfect sense to me (each character and the cards she/he can use create a great synergy). 

I enjoy the way the AI is structured and how the story flows smoothly and "reads" by itself. Personally, I'm very fan of H. P. Lovecraft works, so this kind of "choose-your-destiny" game provides a very special flavor to my taste. 

Tons of love and respect for this! Well, except for FFG's merchandise model: why the heck couldn't they (economic interests of course) include 2 copies of each faction player card and charge us 5 extra bucks? This is beyond my capacity of understanding/tolerance. 

Anyway, I'm crazy for this game, so all I can tell you is: if you have addiction tendencies, put yourself away of this game!

RATING: 4
LCG - should stand for LIMITED card game because that's what it says about the replay-ability in the absence of any expansions.

I mean, contrast this with Race for the Galaxy that gets a lot of flak for being difficult to learn. This is much harder to learn and when you've finally got the hang of it you've already played through the short three scenario campaign. You can try playing through it again to see if you can do any better, maybe try another character ,but you already know the story. No two games of RFtG are alike; there's so much more replay-ability even with only the base game.

Admittedly, this is really the antithesis of the sort of game I like; thematic and story driven but with complex, fiddly rules and incredibly dull mechanics. Don't even bother with this unless you plan to buy into the franchise and get lots of additional content to keep it fresh.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
This game is great!  I can't say enough good things.  you will want to replay missions multiple times.

You do have to enjoy building the decks to get the most out of it and in order to get the most out of building decks you will need to invest in two core sets and all the expansions.  Which, I want to be clear here, I have no problem with the game having expansions, but essentially forcing players to buy two core sets simply to get 20 (necessary) cards is really pretty lame of FFGs part.  No reason they couldn't... do a simple conversion kit.

I am not usually looking for this much of a time/money investment from a game.  Fortunately, my friend already made the investment and I just get to play.  EDIT: I just made a trade for this game from said friend.  Now I own it!  Very excited.

And it exceeded my expectations.

This is not an RPG in a box.  It's most definitely a living card game where a lot of fun is in making a good deck regardless of any ideas you might have about your character.  Having said that, the scenarios are very thematic and evocative, but you are not roleplaying (although you will get invested in your character).

In a weird way, it's sort of a better Elder Sign where the Yahtzee-style dice system is replaced with an LCG.

It's also what the Pathfinder ACG wanted to be, but so much tighter and more thematic.

Honestly, if you like 2 out of 3 on Cthulhu, cooperative games, and LCGs, check it out!  But if you can afford to get (all of) it, I don't blame you.

For all of my thoughts see my video review!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
„Újabb csápbőrbe kötött lapok”
Lovecraft könyveinek, mítoszának FFG játékokba öntött világa kapott egy újabb „gyermeket”, „pénznyelőt”. Akinek az LCG rövidítés, deck-bulding kifejezés mond valamit, az tudja köbö mire számíthat.
Egy biztos, a minőséggel nincs probléma. A kártyajáték lapjai gyönyörűek, szinte külön élmény ilyen játékot a kézbe venni.
A korábban megismert nyomozók köszönnek vissza ránk az új játék mechanikájának megfelelő statisztikával, képességekkel.
A játékban a kártyák jelképeznek (szinte) mindent. Forgatókönyveket, felvonásokat, helyszíneket, tárgyakat, varázslatokat, szövetségeseteket, ellenségeket, a mítosz negatív hatásait. Mellé kapunk jelölőket gazdagon és káosz jelzőket is, amelyek garantáltan megkeserítik a játék során életünket (egyben a kockadobás véletlenszerűségét hivatottak itt megjeleníteni).
Az adott küldetés előkészítése után kezünkbe vesszük kiválasztott nyomozónk meghatározott darabszámú és felépítésű (pakliépítés indul) pakliját és felhúzzuk kezünket (esetleg cserélünk pár lapot).
Első kör kivételével a Mítosz visszavág. Kihívások, nehézségek, ellenfelek. Utána végre lélegzethez jutunk és akciózunk, amelynek keretében 3 akciót hajtunk végre (lehetnek azonosak is): felszerelésünk használata, erőforrások beszerezése, lap felhúzása, harc. Majd következnek a pályán lévő ellenségeink és utána frissülünk. Közben figyeljük, hogyan is haladunk az adott kaland/kampány céljával.
Érdekesség, hogy egyes lapok lehetővé teszik, hogy elmeneküljünk a kalandból, mivel kampány módban a nyomozó halálának következménye is van.
A játékban megjelenik a koop jelleg is, így az egy helyszínen lévő nyomozók lap eldobásával megsegíthetik kis társaikat a nagyobb jó érdekében.
A kaland végén átélt (vagy inkább túlélt) élmények tapasztalati pontot is jelentenek, amivel nekifoghatunk a paklinkba megszerezni a fejlettebb, erősebb lapokat.
Negatívum(ok)ként elmondható, hogy a játék szabályai bár egyszerűnek tűnnek, sok apróságot figyelembe kell venni és már kiadásra került hibajegyzék is. Szóval „szabály-heavy” a játék. Természetesen a szövegezés angol nyelvű és bármilyen magyar fordítás is keletkezik, a játék közbeni ide-oda lapozgatás az élmény rovására megy. Kinek mi, de ha megtetszik a játék, akkor már készítheted is a pénztárcádat, hiszen az alapkészlettel sem tudsz igazi pakliépítésbe fogni, plusz az egyes kalandok (scenario) és kampányok is unalomba fulladnak többszöri végigjátszáskor (persze már most létezik több rajongók által készített jobb-rosszabb kalandok, kampányok, mivel népszerű a játék). A játék bár 2 fővel jól pörög, 3-4 fővel már rá kell szánni azt a 2 órát.
Számomra kettős az „élmény”. Külalakban nincs hiány, kihívás is van gazdagon, lehet nyelvet gyakorolni és – főleg – pénztárcát nyitogatni.

LCG de ambientacion lovecraftiana.

Por supuesto, al ser LCG, ya se sabe lo que hay: ciclos de expansiones a saco.

en mi caso, pendiente de primera partida.

RATING: 10
The game reminds me a lot of the Lord of the Rings Living Card Game, but Arkham Horror LCG has slightly simpler rules and an outstanding scenario design. Up to now I've played the scenarios from the base box and the first five scenarios of The Dunwich Legacy. All scenarios had mechanisms that suited the story they tell. Recording things in your campaign log nicely ties different scenarios together.

I recommend playing the easy difficulty. I think making all checks harder doesn't add much to the game.

There are also a few things I dislike:
I don't like that the rules are split into a learn to play guide and a rules reference. The learn to play guide is good for the first scenario. After that you have to be careful not to forget to look up monster abilities in the rules reference, if you haven't encountered them yet. And I don't think there are people who like to read an alphabetically ordered rules reference from begin to end.

The Dunwich Legacy box is a cheap thin paper box instead of a real box like the Arkham Horror LCG box itself. Since the prices of the add-on scenarios are not too cheap, I think they could have afforded real boxes.

RATING: 9
Light rpg Style Lcg game, with elements from Lotr Lcg and Pathfinder adventures.
Your character is decaying while you deck is upgrading thought the series of small scenarios.
Theme is there, the deckbuilding is meaningfull, you really have to plan your turn, how to best manage your hand and resources in any given situation.
The game is very playable even after several plays.

Definitely hard to learn if you Are not familiar with card games where you build your deck before playing the game, Also the limited deck upgrades between scenarios can be hard to pure card gamers, this is more like one deck to rule emm all Style game.

But if you like card games and Also like roleplaying games, this game is perfect to you!

I personally own four cores, two dunwitch legasy, two miscatonic university etc... so I really have room to build deck just like I want them to be.
The game is like all co-ops hard. So far eleven games of core campaigns at standard difficulty and two victories. So this is definitely easier than Ghost Stories or Robinson Crusoe, but not too easy neither. And if the games seems to be too easy or hard, it is easy to tweak the level by modifuing the chaos back. Very clever system indeed!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Opinions based on playing the base game (2 core sets) and the Dunwich Legacy cycle a few times.
Game play is ok, nothing spectacular. It feels to me like a "Story before mechanisms" sort of game.  Unfortunately, I'm a "mechanisms before story" kind of person.

The randomness in the game can be really frustrating some times. The game rarely lets you feel like you accomplish things based on your prioritizations and decisions. Action results are often at the whims of the token draws. Failure is costly (in terms of actions, health, sanity and other resources), but even more so if you tried to mitigate the token draw but failed.
The game doesn't really have much progression built into the mechanisms. Most cards played into the tableau have a limited amount of uses, making most feelings of engine building and card combo plays short-lived.

The best thing about the game is the campaign structure. Scenarios can have multiple endings, each affecting the coming scenarios in different ways. There are no clear win/lose conditions, but some outcomes are obviously better than others. This really helps brings out the story and increases the immersion.

The deck building before each campaign is good (once you have at least the complete first cycle). It lets you toy with different game concepts depending on what type of character you will be playing. It's nice to work out a plan for which cards shall be in your deck in the beginning and which cards you're aiming to acquire during the campaign.
Upgrading cards between scenarios doesn't feel as interesting as I would have hoped. It's mostly about upgrading cards to other cards that does the same thing but better. I would have preferred a more flexible style of deck-building in mid campaign. Allowing you to redo a scenario with a tweaked deck.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The greatest of all LCGs. Thematic, challenging, horror/adventure RPG, in the form of a card game. One of the best solo games out there.  Oozes theme (and the theme is HPL...).
It is not for the faint of heart, though...both in-game and out of it.
In-game because in each scenario you will run against the clock to undo the evil plans of otherworldly entities and their human cultists, and at every step you will need to test your mettle against nigh impossible odds... and against the Chaos Bag....
And it is not for the faint of heart for another reason: it will take a lot of money from you.
But it is irrelevant: when you will finish a game you will wish to have more.... more scenarios, more player cards, more investigators.... and luckily you will have all of it. For a long time.

RATING: 5
So far I have only played through the first scenario in the box, but this has been excellent after a faltering start. Theme is excellent, the idea of creeping doom is easily realised, and the mechanics around using the tokens in an obscured bag is also a welcome change. You should consider buying a wee pouch bag for this! I have now bought a couple of expansions, and will continue to play this in solo mode.

Edit: 6/11/17
After initially liking this, I am now finding it unenjoyable. I think it is around the difficulty. I am better off selling this with the expansions rather than abandoning it in my shelves!

Edit 26/11/17
I've worked it out - I don't like the random element where you draw cardboard tokens out of a bag as a modifier, and how many negative results there are - it makes the game unnecessarily hard.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
While I still prefer its spiritual predecessor in Lord of the Rings: The Card Game, I am an enthusiastic fan of this game. FFG took the LotR concept, tweaked it for theme and (admittedly) smoothed over some minor gameplay issues to make a very thematic and enjoyable experience. 

The artwork is gorgeous and definitely evokes the other games in the Arkham Files universe. 

I only have two quibbles with it at the moment. The first is that the rulebook(s) can be somewhat confusing. My group had to reference it pretty consistently throughout the first play. A few vague areas as well took me to the messageboards, and one rule in particular concerning how monsters Spawn was not intuitive. 

The second is that the game screams for expansions for deck building, and I almost wish I would have waited a few months and a larger card pool to begin. 

All in all, recommended.

RATING: 7.5
My enjoyment of this game has grown over time, although I still have no plans to go 'all in'. It is an ambitious effort, with some great gameplay ideas and rich thematic ingredients. I increasingly appreciate aspects like the variability of the Encounter Deck and extra story-telling layer brought by campaign-play. However, features like the overused draw bag and constrained sense of investigator location/movement, dilute the immersiveness of the experience. Casual players, like myself, may also find the rule set tiringly dense. Still, I have enjoyed some very exciting sessions and anticipate revisiting AH LCG from time-to-time through the standalone scenario packs.

RATING: 9
Fantastic game. Dripping with theme, and captures the setting perfectly. Almost as fun to play solo as with another investigator. I wish it was a little easier to play with more than two, but you need at least another core set. The rules are fairly easy to pick up, and set up is not too bad either. I wish I got it to the table more often but we usually have a player count higher than 2. The stories are engrossing and the sense of discovery and hidden danger is real. I'd recommend this to almost anyone, even if you are not a Cthulu fan.

RATING: 9
One of the better meshes between game and story that I've played so far. But like many linearly based story games, I'm wondering if I'd be interested in the game long term. I'm not sure the mechanisms really hold up, as I feel the game is, by design, pretty unfair. The story element is strong enough to shore up my frustrations I would normally reserve for a game like this after one run through the campaign.

I'm very irritated at the design of the game that you can't really effectively deck-build with only one core set though. That's such a slimy system in my opinion. It feels like an incomplete product. Something I know many are accustomed to, but I am not, and generally have held back on games like this that encourage that sort of behavior.

RATING: 9
2 Cores. Fantastic game - the narrative is excellent, but there is plenty of room for good (and bad) play, so it delivers on both the experience and the strategy front. The cards having dual purpose (play them or use them to boost skills) means that almost every turn provides meaningful decisions. The replay value is great - first time through a scenario is a ride, a wild one - like driving with your eyes closed, and in the same way, it will likely cause you to crash. Subsequent plays are much more strategic, since you know what's coming - but randomised events and locations keep it from becoming stale. Highly recommended if you like co-operative, customisable card games. On the negative side, it is an expensive game to keep up with (like all LCGs/CCGs), but it is very worth it.

RATING: 9
[2017.Mar.30] This is an intriguing amalgamation of an LCG and an RPG. A much better co-op game than FFG's [i]The Lord of the Rings: The Card Game[/i] and better designed for replayability. Intentionally slow deck tuning of the course of a campaign (multiple scenarios), as opposed to hyper-deck tuning associated with so many other card games, makes the game less about having the perfect cards and more about playing what you have. The game does an excellent job of conveying the mystery & helplessness so often associated with Mythos stories... you don't win, you survive.

RATING: 7.3
Played the first scenario twice. Once on easy and again on hard. It's quite punishing.

The dice rolling ("bag of chaos") is the worst aspect of the game, putting a massive RNG spin on everything, in a cumbersome token-drawing bag. This is in-line with other existing Arkham Horror titles out there, but I was hoping the card game would move away from the luck-based nature of other titles. It is not AS random as Arkham Horror the board game, but does hit many of the same notes.

The narrative and scenario design is clever. The card designs too. Well designed overall.  Will update on subsequent plays.

[update] Sold this off after dying twice on the final encounter. I actually love that the game is challenging and you're not guaranteed to win, however, I had the distinct impression that you had to just "keep trying" until getting the correct lucky card draws and monster spawns - similar to how the Arkham Horror board game is ultimately made or broken by the RNG elements as well. With the deck customization absolutely minimal (basically choosing card A, B, or C for each deck!), I lost the sensation that I was succeeding or failing based on our decisions and strategy. 

Gf still loved the game and was mad I sold it, lol.

RATING: 9
My first LCG.

Pros:
- Building an initial card deck and then spending XP between scenarios to tweak it is great
- Using cards/skills to counter your opposition stacked with the random draw (token bag) is exciting and feels like you have "some" control over the luck
- Exploring a scenario for the first time is thrilling
- The unique investigator strengths/weaknesses means there's a fair bit of variety to encourage replay

Cons:
- Core package is very stingy -- no cloth bag, no storage baggies, insert not suitable for any expansion
- Core package only has one campaign (playable in three parts)

RATING: 10
I waited to rate the game until enough content has been released. This rating is based on my plays up to the third scenario in The Path to Carcosa cycle. I am rating this game a 10 based on the enjoyment I have experienced playing it, and it is the game I want to play the most. If you only own the core set, it's a 8 or 9 rating based on the design and narrative. Start with the core set and if you enjoy the narrative and mechanics, and are having fun, purchase the Dunwich Legacy cycle. The extra content really makes this game better. Since this is a expandable card game, my rating could change based on future content. 
You can have a lot of fun with a core set and even more with another cycle, either Dunwich Legacy or The Path to Carcosa. There are some negatives, the game is rather expensive if you want to continue the narrative and want the extra player cards in the expandable packs. The game also requires you to purchase two core sets if you want to play 3-4 players (1-2 can play with one core set). I really think you need two core sets just to get doubles of player cards in the core set. With further expansions this may be unnecessary if the player cards in the core set can be replaced by cards in the expansions. The only argument I have against the expense is that this is a cooperative game, so you only need to expand as you wish. You do not need expand to compete as in a competitive game. You may chose which content to purchase on your own schedule  This is the game I wished Lord of the Rings Card Game was, as I enjoy the Lord of the Rings theme better.
