=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The ultimate board game for me would probably involve a very strong adventure feel, with ideally character progression, each game having a strong narrative and not feeling samey from the previous yet having high replayability, scale well solo, retain a good amount of decision making, and not require lots of metagaming. 

LOTR LCG failed in this regard due to having heavy metagaming, not scaling well solo, and the need to construct anew destroying any sense of progression. The D&D adventure board games failed due to weak progression, and each scenario being a batch of random monsters before a final boss fight killing off a narrative. Mice and Mystics failed by having too little decisions and limited replayability. Warhammer Quest Adventure Card Game had the most potential and had as a bonus some unique coordination between heroes needed, but the loss of IP along with the core set feeling more mechanical than narrative made it fall. Mansions of Madness had terrible replayability, and remembering spoilers felt more important than strategy in its decision making during repeated plays.

My first play of Arkham Horror LCG it seemed like I had come closest to this "ultimate game" (well not as my favorite game, but the best implementation in the genre of my "ultimate game) I've ever been. How the game sets you up from the get-go really gives the feeling of hopelessness that I would expect from this genre, and the different scenario resolutions with mysterious text to write down in your campaign notes, promises of pseudo-legacy effects (without overdoing it with the difficulty of balancing legacy games) really aided the narrative. The XP system seemed to promise on character progression, and the lack of reconstructing your deck after each scenario seemed to aid in both character progression and not having to metagame. The different encounter decks per scenario (also one of the strong suits of LOTR LCG) made each scenario feel different even prior to the climax, and the physical moving between locations felt better than the more abstracted locations of LOTR LCG or WQ:tACG. The fact you'll draw different starting hands each game and that there are different hero classes with completely different decks promised replayability. There weren't any scenario-specific things that didn't seem to scale poorly solo.

However, future plays made me enjoy the game significantly less. There are some resolutions that are near impossible (and one that is actually impossible if a certain card is drawn). Now that doesn't mean the game is near impossible to win for certain characters, but at the end of the first scenario the resolution choices also made it seem like the game would have diverging narratives based on your past actions. The more straitjacketed narrative isn't really a flaw per se, but  is more that I thought the game would be head and shoulders above its peers in narrative, and later found out it only executes about the same level.

The cards in the coreset are very limited in deck building. Even if you were intentionally trying to replace as many cards as possible I think you would only be able to change out a third of each character's deck compared to the recommended set. The poor deck building is exacerbated by the fact that certain cards are really really important to have early (with not enough options to sift through your deck for them), and that some cards are really poor unless combo'd with other cards or character powers, but you need to include such poor cards with the characters that don't have such combos due to card requirements. Clearly this issue will be alleviated with expansions, but how much do I need to get into this game before this aspect is fixed? Also the XP system is a lot weaker than I thought it would be, not just because there aren't that many card options to buy, but also you are maybe replacing 10% of your deck after each scenario. With maybe 60% of cards drawn per scenario, that is less than 2 cards changed per game. This does mean it is possible to play scenarios out of order however.

The different scenarios did feel fairly different from each other, however I didn't like how the second scenario and non-hunter monsters felt more like you were coming at the game rather than the game coming at you. This is a non-issue for Mage Knight fans, but for someone like me who likes the never ending swarm that Ghost Stories throws at you, this made the second scenario feel less tense.

The different characters do in fact have a fairly different play style, for example Roland thrives off of picking off weak monsters while Wendy needs to constantly dance around to avoid them. My favorite character is probably Agnes due to her utility with her spells and mitigating their often negative horror infliction (that make the spells almost bad for other characters) a positive thing, while my least favorite is probably Daisy, who is way to reliant on getting a good tome out quickly. There is also enough replayability to play the same scenario with the same deck and character after awhile.

While the tokens generally scale based on number of players, there are some plot-related things in the second and third scenario that have no scaling whatsoever. I'm not necessarily opposed to some differences in difficulty for different player counts, but the blatancy of this just makes it lazy design. Also, the lack of having another character to synergize with really hurts and makes the game seem like it has the swingy encounter cards that LOTR LCG has. For example, there is one monster that basically doubles the doom placed each round unless defeated. With 2 players one hero is bound to be able to have enough strength to defeat it, but if you are soloing with Wendy have fun having your available time cut in half, unless you just so happen to have the card that lets you remove a monster you are evading. Again, this might be less of an issue with expansions.

A lot of the luck mitigation of this game also seems to having a full hand size so you can boost your stats when doing checks. However, one of the weaknesses discards your cards, making it hard to mitigate, and the second scenario and the much more feasible resolution for the 3rd scenario depending on which character you are playing are basically races. This makes the luck of your starting hand much more apparent as you don't have enough time to spend actions to constantly keep your hand well stocked.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 3
[Played 4 times]

It's horribly shallow.

Tokens are just there for the novelty factor (they would be like cards, but they are always returned to the bag so they are just dice; pro: you can customize the faces of the dice during setup to set your chances to win higher or lower as desired; con: it is annoyingly longer and clumsier than rolling dice).

Deckbuilding does not exist (you'll need expansions for that).

Strategy is as banal as it gets (you just specialize in one aspect - fighting, investigating, etc. - and keep doing that over and over).

The "choose your own adventure" elements try to give it a narrative (that would like to be Lovercraftian to boot...), but mostly manage to limit the replayability.

RATING: 9.6
After going through the campaign a second time, I wanted to revise a few points, though my opinion of the game only improved.  I still think that unless you want an RPG-like story this game is not for you.  However, there is more game here than was obvious at first.  The deck building and multiple use of cards creates tense and interesting decision before the game, when you build your deck, and during the game, when you try to decide how to best use each card.  Replay value is better than I thought.  Although the main story points remain the same, the obstacles create different situations and the scenarios play out differently each time.  The main story, which is formulaic predictable, is the weakest part of the game anyway.  The details of how the main story points are resolved is interesting and engaging, making this game shine.

RATING: 5
Oh dear! After 11 plays I have lost both my starting Investigators: Roland retired with too many Mental Traumas to be worth playing and Wendy was Killed [o]by a Scenario outcome[/o]

It's really shades of LotR all over again: bad card draws and you're fucked. In LotR this was a ball-ache, but you could reset and go again. But in AHTCG you're playing a scenario-based campaign so you can't go back and do it over, you carry that failure into the next Scenario and the next while it all gets worse and worse.

I'm going to try the starter scenario with all the Investigators in the hope that one of them will win through. But the problem is I'll then take that Investigator into the next Campaign and lose while trying to learn [I]that[/I] campaign. So then I'll have to learn the new Campaign with new Investigators. Essentially I see any given Investigator as being good only for one Campaign. 

At this point it looks like I'll do the Dunwich cycle and then bail - if I don't bail before the end.

Update @17 plays. This one has gone the way of LoTR: I love the concept, but I'm not motivated to put in the effort to figure out a good deck to play an Investigator with. Added to that I don't like having multiple runs through a campaign game, it makes it less like a campaign than I wanted. It's a well executed game design that I just don't happen to like. It'll go on the "For Trade" heap.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
In short:
I really did look forward to play this game and really did want to play it. But the incompleteness of rules and lack of effort in card descriptions just made me feel that this game was way too rushed and not polished at all.

Who do I recommend to?
If you are eager for an RPG like board game, with very loosely interpreted complex rules with a heavy entry barrier and don't really care to play by the book. (aka. know exactly how the rules should be interpreted)

Who do I don't recommend it to?
Anyone who have played magic the gathering or similar complex game with a desire to know how the cards exactly work.

Pros:
- You can really play it in an RPG fashion and have stay-in-character conversations.
- The game can really surprise you in many ways
- It really does have a blend of darkness if you are open for RPG
- Many unique cards
- Very good card and image quality
- Custom deckbuilding before play

Cons:
- Movement is way too much restricted the campaign is very linear
- Once you know the locations themselves, there is no surprise factor, so it's lot less fun to play a particular mission again.
- Long and elaborate game setup before play. Had to pick my entire deck one by one matching tiny numbers that could have been printed a lot bigger.
- Card descriptions are hard to decipher. They are sometimes too short and vague to understand, despite plenty of space for text on the card.
- Rules Reference has lot of concepts missing. Half of the keywords or things I tried to look up was missing from there.
- The game feels incomplete and unpolished. The main rule of thumb is that if you can't find a rule, just apply the way it's affects you the worst way. Which is a terrible excuse for missing rules, especially that it wasn't even applicable for the situations.
- It is definitely longer than 2 hours, even if you are experienced, I can't see it being just an hour. Unless you manage to loose early on somehow - maybe on hard difficulty.
- On top of the basic situations are poorly documented, there are many exceptions or edge cases that take a lot of effort to work out.
- Punishing weaknesses that you can do nothing about. Ex: throw away all your cards - which actually meant loosing 7 cards from a full hand!!!

Deckbuilding:
- I didn't get to the deck-building phase, but it looks like that might give a bit of an additional fun, if you are a really big fan of the game.

RATING: 6
Arkham Horror, this is not. 

This is yet another game that tries to capture the feel of a Lovecraftian story. This time, in card game form. Even though I went into this with some trepidation, I wanted to give it a try since Arkham Horror is my favorite board game. For the most part, I thought the game was decent. If nothing else, it flowed very smoothly, and the drawing chits out of a bag, while sometimes frustrating, was a different approach to the same old skill checks these games are known for. It works well enough, but I don't see why they needed to make this when it would have been just as easy to come up with more scenarios for Mansions of Madness. If I'm going to play a game with this theme, I want it to either be epic (Arkham Horror), tense (Mansions of Madness), or story-driven (Mythos Tales). This game doesn't really accomplish any of those things any better than what has come before. This is just Fantasy Flight's attempt at cashing in on yet another IP that has sold well in the past. Seriously...if you want a solo game with this theme, any of the other games that have already come out are just as good as this, if not better.

RATING: 10
The ongoing campaign format, with replayable scenarios released monthly, is EXACTLY the type of game I have always wanted.  The strategy of deck building and the decision making during the super creative mechanics BOTH dripping with great artwork and theme make this a complete package of gaming greatness.  It gets my second "10" joining Space Hulk - Death Angel as a favorite game.  
[B]TTS Play:[/B] Started with starter deck solo plays of the intro scenario with base box investigators.  Roland Banks managed to score 4 Victory Points by clearing out the Attic and the Cellar as well as defeating the Ghoul Priest.  It was a blast, and perhaps a bit lucky with respect to the Mythos effects.  The Ghoul Priest was taken out with a clutch stick of dynamite (after a timely resource boost) and a forceful knife jab (buffed by a skill modifier card).  FFG did not disappoint and now I am all in on the entire line.  Second play was with the challenging soloist, Daisy.  I had the PERFECT plan lined up to take out the Ghoul Priest (Mind over Matter and two knife attacks with resource buffed traits).  Instead, I drew Stubborn Detective and the d-bag foiled everything.  Great story.  Fun game.  Play three was even more tense as little Wendy tried desperately to take out the Ghoul Priest.  She had one shot, and failed (both draws)!  So close!  Play four left Skids with one horror and a dead Ghoul Priest, thanks again to dynamite and a buffed knife strike (3 VPs).  Really nice token pulls with Agnes.  She missed EVERY time with Shriveling, but with her ability and a baseball bat, she took the fight to the Ghoul Priest FTW (3 VPs).

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
I have thought a lot about this game since I first played it and finally decided to rate it. It is a 2 for me. If you are reading my review, keep in mind that I normally play euros and this is my first LCG. 
There are lots of things to like: the art is excellent. The story is not bad, too (seems to be much better thought out than Time Stories Asylum, but it probably is due to Lovecraft's established universe, not the designers.) The card quality is good, too. 

Unfortunately, nothing else in the game per se worked for me. 

First of all, it is very luck based. You get a cup with tokens and basically all you do when you fight or evade enemies is compare the skill level you have at the moment to that of the monster you're fighting and add a random modifier token from the cup which can hurt you or help you. That's basically it. That's the whole game. To mitigate this luck factor you are encouraged to build your own better deck (usually by buying extra packs of cards). So, yes, you can mitigate this luck a little bit by building a 'stronger' deck, but all in all, it boils down to 'roll a 6, defeat your enemy', 'roll a 1, try again'.  I really don't get how people can say that, for instance, card draws in Agricola are luck based and totally glaze over when it comes to luck here. Agricola is Go compared to Arkham Horror the card game. 

Still, all this luckfest could have been ok, honestly. I mean when I go see a summer blockbuster in a theater I do it to have fun. I don't overanalyze and actively search for plot holes. It's sheer mindless fun that you have for 2 hours and then forget about. This Arkham Horror game could have served the same purpose. Unfortunately, it doesn't, because to me, the system is quite convoluted and you have to invest a lot into studying how various cards work together, how enemies work etc. To me, it's just not worth it. I would have played it just to see where the story takes me if it were an app and all the upkeep was done for me, but as it is there are too many other great boardgames to waste your time learning all the nooks and crannies of this system.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10
Incredible game, my wife and I have played through the core and the Dunwich campaigns as well as the Curse of the Rougarou and Carnival of Horrors stand alones. 

I have not had any problems owning a single core set, as I primarily enjoy working through the stories, rather than min / maxing my decks. Now that I have most of two cycles worth of expansions, there are plenty of options for upgrades. 

This is currently a 10/10 game for me, and I look forward to each new mythos pack as it is released. Its not a game I can play every game night, but when we do play it I enjoy every minute of it.

RATING: 7.5
規則:
*把對應的16個混亂標記丟到袋中，選一張戰役卡，拿對應的場景卡(有對應符號的特定牌組)，把議程卡照數字跟英文字小到大疊起，並根據1a處理劇情，同樣設置設置行動卡，該戰役的場景參考卡放一旁，將書房放入(有鑰匙孔面)，其他會使用的地點卡放一旁，將玩家腳色小卡放在書房旁，書房翻面，幾個玩家就放置左邊線索數幾組，剩下的場景卡全部洗勻
*每個人選擇一個腳色，並選一個人當主導者，根據腳色洗勻起始牌組(使用左上沒有升級標記的牌)，每個人拿五個資源標記，然後抽五張牌，如果抽到弱點牌，先放一旁繼續抽，只後洗進去(每個人可重抽特定張數一次，把最多五張放一旁在抽到五張，放一旁得洗進去)
*任何規則處理如果不確定都傾向於對玩家造成最大傷害的選項
*遊戲主要目的是要結算行動卡，並進入下張，如果議程卡被結算通常是負面的，玩家全部死亡也要看參考卡來講述結局
*照順位時由主導玩家開始順時鐘結算，調查員頭像代表該數值要乘上玩家數
*要做技能測試時，從混亂袋中抽一個標記，跟調查員該技能加總，超過或等於就成功，在抽前可棄牌使用左上標記增加該技能數值，測試玩家可用多張，其他同地點玩家一張，或是使用調查員能力，結算完後所有出的牌棄掉，混亂標記丟回袋中
*玩家如果抽到弱點卡就馬上結算
*遊戲進行數輪，每輪有四個階段
1.神話階段(第一輪跳過)
>放一個末日標記在議程卡上
>比較末日標記數目跟議程卡左下數字，超過或等於時結算該卡，翻面結算開下一張，末日標記棄掉
>照順位每人抽一張遭遇卡並結算之，敵人卡直接遭遇該腳色(除非有重生點敘述)，叛變卡結算
2.調查階段  玩家自行決定行動順序，每個調成員有三個行動點，行動如下，如果遭遇敵人的話，執行戰鬥逃離跟戰鬥相關能力以外的行動會導致被敵人攻擊
>抽一張牌
>獲得一個資源
>啟動一個花行動的能力，可能在各種卡片上
>遭遇一個同地點的敵人，將敵人放在自己的威脅區(可遭遇在隊友威脅區的)
>調查該地點 做一次書本測試，總數值大於該地點左邊數字則成功，拿一個線索標記
*花線索來完成行動卡不算是行動，所有玩家可以一起支付，在任意玩家回合都可以做，行動卡翻面結算後打開下一張
>移動到相鄰的地點 地點相鄰與否看卡牌的下方對應卡牌的左上，到為探索過的地點就翻開，放對應線索標記
*如果移動時有敵人在威脅區，敵人會跟著過去，每個地點只要有未橫置的敵人都會馬上遭遇該地點的調查員，多人則領導者決定
>出一張資產或事件卡 支付卡片左上費用，事件卡結算後棄掉，資產卡放腳色旁，有迅速特性的卡不花行動，腳色除手跟秘笈可裝兩張以外，其他一張，不夠格則棄掉一張
>逃離一個遭遇的敵人 做一次敏捷測試，對應敵人卡最右邊數值，成功敵人卡橫置放到腳色地點卡旁
>跟同地點敵人戰鬥 攻擊同地點的敵人，使用拳頭測試，成功敵人受到一點傷害(敵人卡中間是血量)，傷害超過血量則敵人卡棄掉，有分數的保留一旁，如果失敗且該敵人跟另一位調查員遭遇，則該調查員受一點傷害
3.敵人階段
>未橫置的有獵人特性的敵人往有調查員的地點移動一格，如果遇到調查員馬上遭遇之
>所有在威脅區的敵人做一次攻擊，使用卡片下方的血量跟恐懼值，放到對應腳色上，攻擊完的卡橫置，如果有資產上面有血量也可放傷害，放滿就要棄掉
4.維持階段
>所有調查員卡重置，橫置卡也轉正，所有未遭遇的敵人卡如果地點有調查員就遭遇之
>每個調查員抽一張牌跟獲得一個資源
>手牌超過八張的棄掉
****組牌規則***
*選腳色，腳色有卡牌張數限制，弱點，調查員專屬卡跟場景卡不再此限
*只能選角色卡後面有的卡單，每種卡最多兩張，戰役模式時只能包含等級0的卡
*基礎弱點卡洗勻抽一張洗進去
*卡片等級從左上數字圓環內的白點個數看
***戰役規則***
*使用對應的幾張場景卡，根據選擇的難易度放對應的混亂標記到袋中
*根據對應場景設置並進行遊戲，根據戰役內容結算最後結果
*進入下個場景前可用經驗值買道具或能力，要保持牌庫大小，有些卡可升級，花費基本上都是左上白點的差值，買牌也是
*死亡的腳色除非規則特別說 否則就獲得創傷繼續進行到下個場景

RATING: 10
The rubric for a 10 is "Will always enjoy playing." I can't really know if that's the case. If there's anything this game "wants" to teach us, it's that our future is uncertain. Maybe one day I'll suddenly hate it, won't be able to stand the sight of it; but for now my favorite part of this game isn't even playing it -- it's introducing other people to it. I love DMing this game. Though strictly speaking it doesn't need a DM, the game goes more smoothly when someone is running it for you, paying attention to the rules, the turn order, the corner cases. Most of all I love watching people get into the mood of the game as I load up a spooky playlist and then read the scenario and agenda cards to them. I love celebrating the lucky pulls, agonizing over the unlucky draws with them.

The fact I can play this game solo is a huge bonus for me. The buy-in is pretty real, but the fact that I haven't regretted the game's cost is a huge testament to how enjoyable the game is. I've got two core sets, the base Dunwich box and its first two expansion packs, and finally the Rougarou POD. That's nearly $200 so far. You know what? To make room in my budget, I'll eat out less for this game. I will eat some basic-ass sandwiches for a couple days just to pick up the next pack.

I get mad at this game. I get mad at this game the same way I get mad at, say, Rocket League or Overwatch. When I get a run of bad luck in this game, it feels like something is actively working against me. Like something hates me and wants me to fail. When I get exactly the card or token I needed to win, I feel overwhelming relief. The highs are high and the lows are low. I get emotionally involved. I don't feel like that when I play most games.

Arkham Horror makes me feel things.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
It has all the upsides of LCG's, with the challenge and satisfaction they all provide of building a deck that works. It has more thematic narrative upside with linked scenarios providing a campaign feel (although I'd prefer to see even more linkage). 

By way of disclosure, I was involved in early playtesting and assisted with card and rules editing (though they did change further after I’d finished).

I’ve played AH and EH, to which this is thematically linked, but my comparative point of view is more towards other LCG’s as this is the framework from which the game was built. Some will be attracted to the game by the theme despite not being card-heads, others because they love the rich depth and extensive replay that the LCG deck-building world provides, where an Arkham based co-op fits their idea of a fun space to explore. To that end, I have some form, having been a playtester and editor for Star Wars LCG and Warhammer Conquest LCG, and played 500+ games of LotR LCG, which is similarly co-op. 

The deckbuilding is limited with just the core set (as is always the case with LCG’s until you invest in expansion packs), but it gives you enough to tinker with and a few ways to play so as to earn the value of each type of card. As you progress through each scenario, you earn XP and spend them to swap out a card for either a stronger version of that card (typically), or maybe a different stronger card. If you think of your deck as representing your character, then this represents levelling up your character. (In practice, this campaign style is not dissimilar to how we do our LotR campaigns, keeping the same heroes and restricting ourselves to a limited number of deck changes each scenario. It’s just that it’s a forced structure here.)

In terms of gameplay, the aim of the game is spend as many investigate actions as you can to gather clues - getting the requisite number of clues enables the win. The game then throws as many obstacles in the way to limit your opportunities to do so, be it by introducing monsters that need fighting, forcing you to move to different locations for new sources of clues, giving you “weakness” troubles that require actions to clear, and so on. I’ll confess I wasn’t a big fan of the mathy nature of this structure - each turn I ended up reducing the results of the “introduce badness” phase to how many required actions were just added to the quest. But this structure does provide the necessary fuel around the deck-build - what percentage of the deck will you use to help with clue-gathering, what percentage to fighting monsters and clearing the way, etc.

The other element that ended up turning me away from the game was the calculable nature of resolving any task. All tasks - like investigate a clue, or fighting a monster - require you to reach a given target number using icons on your cards. After you’ve determined how many icons to commit, you draw a chit from the bag that adjusts the target number. The bag is a fixed and known quantity with every draw. So by way of example, I currently have in play the same number of icons as the target number. My chances of success might be 3 in 16. If I throw a card from my hand to add 1 more icon, it increases to 7 in 16 say. Throw in another card with an icon and I’m up to 10 in 16. And so on. Every decision is a calculable risk / reward equation. And when you throw enough in to get a 14 in 16 must--win shot and still lose, it’s not the only thing that gets thrown around the table!! 

This mathy-nature led me to losing thematic immersion and was where the game lost its magic for me. I much prefer the non-calculable nature of LotR. But it’s exactly why this game has taken a different approach - it offers a different feel that will appeal to a different set of gamers, hopefully broadening the LCG player base and limiting the cannibalisation of the LotR player base. 

Having said all that, I’m a fan of the LCG genre as it inherently generates interesting and hard decision making both pre and during games, and this iteration is no different. So even though it’s not my preferred taste, I’ll still enjoy it if it hits the table. I know from past LCG experience that the game will broaden and deepen, and the playing experience will grow richer. Combine that with a wildly attractive theme, and I’m sure the game will prove rewarding for those it appeals to and who invest ongoing.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Great that the artwork isn't as recycled as it is for much of the FFG Cthulhu output.
Does not do as good a job of creating a narrative as it could. If each act and stage had more parts that would help.
Although FFG have obviously put lots of effort into the rules, they still suck. There are loads of parts where after a few games I still have no idea what to do or how to use certain cards and assets.
As with most of the recent FFG games, I find the rules overly and unnecessarily complicated. What i mean is that the many fiddly smaller rules just make the game harder to play, breaking the spell and not actually adding any depth (as Nate also did with the LOTR LCG).

4 tries to get through The Gathering. And it got better as I went along but I still spent too long looking for lost rules!

Solo rules are excellent - just a coop run played by as many characters/hands as you want.

Experience was excellent too.

Score downgraded. Some of the individual scenario rules are extremely poorly laid out. You have to play them, reveal spoilers, go to the forums etc. to figure them out. At the prices we pay, you'd think they could get this sorted.
Blood on the Altar
Undimensioned and Unseen totally unclear.
In fact the rules are so poor and annoying that I will probably just finish Carcosa and walk away from this series.

RATING: 8
Played a 4-player scenario of Rou-ga-roux and failed miserably. I enjoyed it a lot. The two experienced players at our table took us through the rules and I found the game quite accessible. But they didn't spoil the experience for me by suggesting how to play, letting me figure it out myself.

RATING: 8
Definitely feels limited with just the base box; my wife and I were surprised that we were restricted to certain pairs of characters since some of their suggested "basic decks" are mutually exclusive if you only have one set of cards. You'll need a full second set if you want to go to 3 or 4 players as well.

That said, I really enjoy my time with this game. The theme feels spot-on; it may not be the true existential horror of Lovecraft, but the sense of oncoming dread that the Doom Counter mechanic provides in its various forms fits the bill quite nicely. It's also fitting that campaign takes into account varying degrees of success and failure for each scenario; victory and mastery aren't necessary for progress, and that's absolutely welcome in a horror-themed game.

There's definitely a learning curve with plenty of rules references for your first round or two, but we were able to pick it up fairly quickly and on-the-fly. It doesn't grind to a halt if you fudge a rule here or there while you get your bearings. 

I wouldn't begin to understand how to meaningfully optimize deckbuilding, but to me that's a selling point; I can craft a deck from a sense of story and character instead of number-crunching.

RATING: 7
A little disappointed with this game so far.  It's got some things in common with LotR: Card Game, but on the other hand I hate the drawing tokens out of the bag.  You can just pull the really crappy tokens out of the bag over and over again and it just ends up taking all of the fun out of it.

RATING: 9
Setup is slightly more tedious to LotR LCG. There is more story aka flavour text in this game. 

The chaos tokens bag presents another "probability management" problem for skill checks.

However, each player can choose their character and deck before the first game to determine which skill checks they are better at. There is also a survivor/generalist class which can cope better with chaos tokens and failed skill checks.

Each character deck has their own unique weakness card and a random basic weakness card which follows them throughout the campaign. 

There are certain points of the game where you may need to make decisions which may affect you or your team either during the scenario only or even throughout the whole campaign.

RATING: 10
Long I've been awaiting for a game like this. First of all, I love LCGs, CCGs and the like; secondly, I'm a Mythos junkie - enjoy EH, Elder Sign and some others. Arkham Horror LCG marries both in a game that plays incredibly well solo. 

It's very thematic and replayable (even though some of the narrative surprises wear off after a game or two), it's also tense, especially with just one investigator. The classes are done incredibly well, each of them plays differently, making the game a completely new puzzle when you change the investigator. Deck building is fun and not too taxing, the experience mechanic gives a fine RPG feel. The scenarios are different, each feels unique, and the storytelling is great (way above LotR LCG). 

If you look for a game you can play solo in ~45 minutes (kids, lack of time or gaming partners) this is the way to go. Try it, especially if you enjoy Cthulhu games. For me it's a 10, a game I will play for many years to come.

RATING: 7.07
My (hopefully) very last foray into the realm of LCGs. 
I have mixed emotions regarding this game.
It is a fantastic system and the LCG formula gives it a constantly changing / evolving milieu, while financially it remains still affordable. 
But it asks so much from the player: It is a life-style game instead of a game what you can play just occasionally. To fully comprehend its greatness you have to deepdive into deckbuilding, and that requires a lot of time: to construct / test / finetune / play these decks is a serious time commitment, and in my opinion this is the only right way to play these kind of games. If you are commited enough (and if you have the time it requires) this is a deep and satisfying experience. If you don't, then the experience is shallow and only an imitation of the real thing....
So even knowing, that the campaign play is wonderfully realized, that the chaos bag is a fantastic, and that the different investigators and classes each grant a unique playing experience, a bid farewell to this game. It is a 10, but a 10 which I will sell with a broken heart. :(

RATING: 4
I am completely mystified by how well-liked this game is.

After about eight hours of gameplay, it seems like a random, fiddly mess.

It suffers from the same problems as most other so-called Cthulhu/Lovecraft games i.e. decisions are reactive and painfully simple - or would be, but for the distracting  hubbub of ever-changing modifiers, rules exceptions and text.

The only way to 'not play well' is to mis-remember all the different rules that might hurt you.

People say it "tells stories" and even that it has a "roleplaying game" element. Huh? Events are mostly random and usually completely unconnected.

Don't believe the hype about it being a deck-building game, as that element is almost non-existent. You're basically just taking a load of random cards and throwing them together to see if you win.

The cost of expansions is crazy.

I'll play it only because my chum Jon happens to make it fun enough.

Also, have board game writers ever read any H P Lovecraft? His books - and even the slightly sillier writers who followed him - feel nothing like these board games. Instead, the imagery/themes/characters in these board games feel like they should be in a violent first-person-shooter computer game incarnation of Indiana Jones.

The best Cthulhu/Lovecraft game I've played continues to be the first edition of A Study In Emerald (which is different to the second edition). Even that game is a bonkers mess (with a lot of errata too), but there's a lot more to do, a lot less rules to check and more exciting endings.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Greatly enjoy this game!  Very immersive and thematic. Deeply engrossing. And while there is a bit of learning curve, I felt that I had the rules pretty solid by the end of the second scenario.  Looking forward to more content!

[b]Pros:[/b]
- Very thematic!!
- The rulebook is surprisingly well organized. 
- Decently easy to get started as a new player to the genre The game does a good job of providing you with a recommended starter setup without needing any prior deck building knowledge or experience. 
- I like the deck building methods. You can do as little (using the points you get as you progress) or as much (build it from scratch) as you prefer. 
- The 'grim rule' makes it easier to deal with situations where you are not sure how to apply the rules. Helps reduce stalls when a a rules question comes up during play. 
- New content will be available on a semi-regular basis to keep adding new stories, characters, and cards. 
- Great art! 
- The Chaos tokens are a cool way of applying unexpected modifiers. Adds some randomness to your checks in the game and adds suspense. Also makes the game easily adjustable in difficulty. 
-  Adjustable difficulty. 
- Scales decently for 1 up to 4. 
- Enjoyable regardless of player count. 

[b]Cons:[/b]
- Lots of rules and terms to learn. The rulebook makes these really easy to look up but if you're not a fan of lots of rules beware. 
- Will get expensive - as an LCG there will be constantly new expansions and it can get pricy if you want them all. 
- If you want to play with more than 1 or 2 players, you MUST get more than one copy of the game. 
- The bag/cup you need for the Chaos tokens doesn't come with the game. This can be a nuisance for some people. 
- The box is too small!!!  After sleeving I can barely fit 2 core sets in one box. 
- A third party insert is practically necessary because without one setup is quite beastly.
- Set up can be time consuming so if that bothers you beware.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The writing is predictable and dull. A money grab. Gameplay is good, but a bit fiddly.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
This game is perhaps everything I wanted The Lord of the Rings: The Card Game to be. I'm a fan of the Arkham Horror setting, though the only other AH games I own is the FFG version of AH with some expansions. 

The art of course is perfect and fits with the art of the boardgame. Aesthetically it's a very attractive package.

While I own most of the expansions currently out, I've only played the core campaign, though I've used investigators and player cards from expansion. I recommend giving the core campaign a try and then if you enjoy it, going in on the expansions. There's definitely game to enjoy in the core set, but the limiting factor to me is not the three scenario campaign, but the limited pool of player cards. I have not successfully completed to core campaign, but with the increased player cards and investigator pool from the expansions I think there is great replayability with just the short core campaign.

Will come back to this later, but I love the investigator development and customization.

RATING: 9
Love the campaign and the feeling of dread, but I feel this game lives and dies by the deck-building. I enjoyed it a lot more once I got a second core for more options and a binder for organisation. But now that I put more in to my character, I got more upset when I pull bad cards or chaos tokens and ruin my campaign prematurely. It almost feels like you need to know all the act, agenda, and encounter cards in order to craft an effective deck -- I guess that's not bad, but it feels a little bit like gaming the system, and breaks my narrative immersion somewhat.

I'm yet to determine whether the (significant) time and effort I put in to crafting my deck and playing the game is ultimately rewarded by the game systems. So far, it has for the most part.

RATING: 7
First  impressions - interesting blend of LOTR, Pathfinder and Warhammer ACG with some cards and the in-game economy kinda reminiscent of Netrunner. 

Deck building being part of the campaign game is the real selling-point  - the game's strength is that this makes for a strong experience and sense of character and investment - the draw back is the more limited replay  and what inevitably ends up looser as deck-construction  - more about the experience than the strict technical skill involved in play. Liked it a lot, but don't think I'm ready for the investment. Also, after Call of Cthulhu RPG, Arkham Horror, Elder Sign, Mansions of Madness 2, maybe I've seen enough of the theme and these same  protagonists (though i do have an urge to see a Harvey Walters deck). I think I'd like to see this system fitted to a generic fantasy background (or who knows, maybe a non-cyberpunk non-40k SF setting) that allows for endless grinding of quests to keep changing up your deck - though such a design might almost be self-sustaining without need for endless expansions, so I don't expect one anytime soon. AHTCG is really a better game, but (so far!) I think I prefer Pathfinder ACG for campaign and LOTR LCG for the challenge - undoubtedly though this game manages to blend the two elements to achieve something unique from its predecessors. Do really like the art deco-ish card backs, welcome change from the usual loud and messy FFG designs - it's a slick product.

RATING: 10
Edit: After about 50 plays, I've decided to give this game the elusive "10." It's a wonderful game, and I've put more time in to this than most other games/systems. 
I can see myself playing this multiple times a week (or at minimum 1x/week) for the foreseeable future. I cannot say that about any other game, despite how much I enjoy them.  Weird thing is, there are other games I enjoy more and rate lower, but none where I can expect to play it with such consistency. ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~
Original comments, amended with time:
There's a lot that I like here. It's certainly the most interesting of the Arkham Files games, with the largest decision space. The designers have been incredibly creative with scenario building. I have been utterly impressed with the diversity of everything released thus far. Edit after completing DL: This continues to be true, the scenarios remain impressive. 

It's basically a card-driven board game made exclusively of cards- the location cards create a highly malleable setting to explore. 

The deckbuilding is highly approachable and fun. To start, you don't even have the entire card pool to choose from, which makes getting started very easy. The game really opens up over the scenarios.

I love card games, and this one doesn't disappoint. The hand management if fun and feels fresh. 

The game is immersive. And this is someone who doesn't generally care about theme. The pressure is high, and doesn't release. 

My least favorite aspect of the game is the ability to jump around scenarios. Like, if I am playing a scenario with my wife, and then wish to play with my friends, I frequently have to deconstruct my decks in order to make that happen. I suspect that this will be more and more of a minor issue as the card pool expands. Edit after 50 plays: THIS IS STILL MY BIGGEST GRIPE! I have been playing 2 campaigns simultaneously, and I have to share cards between decks. This has not been a deterrent to me, just an annoyance. 

This is easily my favorite couple's game.

RATING: 9
2 sets is almost a must. After that, very good game. The  deckbuilding aspect feels like leveling a character in a RPG. The mandatory weakness cards, as well as the chance to autofail rolls, may seem like a nuisance, but its there to make sure you can never mix-max a scenario, as well as know that this journey is not a normal RPG game where you character becomes a demi-god at the end, but has the scars and lessons from conflict. There is a feeling of real tension is this game I haven't felt in a while in co-op games, there will be many soul-crushing last turn defeats and victories from the jaws of defeat moments. Brilliant game if you give it a chance, creates a bad first impression because of playing with 1 set and the mediocre built in decks.

Best with 2 players in my opinion.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
If LOTR and WQ had a baby... This game adds to the narrative experience and makes exploration fun. While I liked elements of LOTR and WQ I sold both after only a couple of plays each. LOTR felt like an exercise in planning/ going on the Internet and copying other people's tweaked decks (no thanks). WQ was more exciting and felt like a dungeon crawl with no exciting geographical exploration - enemies came from shadows and attached themselves to your characters, it was very abstract.

AH LCG marries the deck building and exciting encounters of these games and vastly improves the way your characters exist in space. you're not floating around somewhere foggy waiting to be attacked, you're deciding to go to the attic to explore and you can only go to the attic from the hallway. You narrowly slipped past the monster that just engaged you (using hand management and push your luck depending on just how important the task at hand is) to find clue in the attic but now you must make your way to the hallway to continue the adventure. When in the hallway you trigger the next part of the narrative which makes sense as you're tied to a location.

The game is very streamlined and the mechanics make perfect sense. The game involves a narrative I care about because the locations aren't abstract - I'm in the lobby because I chose to go there and now a monster is chasing me between the rooms and the card I'm working towards hints at what I must do to get away but oh no something else happened and Ahhhh the cards I just used I should have saved now I need to make a really important skill test. Speaking of skill tests I wanted to like eldritch horror but couldn't get into it as it was more like roller coaster journey on rails than a game with tough decisions.

An excellent evolution it improves both LOTR and WQ while offering meaningful narrative often found in games like eldritch horror yet makes sense while using cards. What an achievement! The card game I've been waiting for!

RATING: 10
Once in a while comes a fantastic game. This, is one. Brilliant system that was already put to the test elsewhere, and it works like magic here. Scenarios and extensions keep rolling, and I just hope that quality follows quantity, and, most importantly, that the game stays as hard as it is right now... It's quite rare that a Lovecraftian game is true to the author's universe and makes you feel helpless, on the edge. As it is, the game forces you to make the right decisions if only to survive until the next turn, rethink your strategy, forge a better deck, instead of becoming, by forgiving poor or sketchy play, the dice fest that its parent game was.

Superb.

RATING: 9
Arkham Horror: The Card Game is the most fun I've had playing a card game solitaire. Takes a little bit of a learning curve to get it down, but the mechanics are solid, the artwork and overall production value is high, and you can't find better lore than H.P. Lovecraft. But once you learn the game, it's so much fun.

RATING: 5
Tried the game a few times solo. Not ready to give up on it yet, but I’m not having as much fun as I thought I would.  

First, there is the hate it or love it Learn to play/Reference rulebook that FFG has adopted, I hate it. I don’t enjoy learning a game this way at all. At it makes reading the rulebook a chore.

Then there is the usual FFG bloat.  For example, there are 20 steps in the round sequence. I found myself having to constantly refer to the two rulebooks to remember the exact details of each step. 

Also, strangely for this type of card game, you could actually play a whole game without playing a single card from your hand. It might not be a good strategy, but it’s entirely possible. It plays more like a boardgame that a card game. 

Everything you do is based on a skill check, and the cards help mitigate the luck. That’s the game. Want to fight? Combat skill + number of icons played - dice rolled (chaos token bag) vs target number. Want to evade? Same thing but with the agility skill. Want to find a clue? Same thing again, different skill.  Etc, etc.

What you are actually trying to do is not that exciting either. Basically as a team you want to gather clue tokens and spend them on the act deck so the story moves forward. And the game throws obstacle at you to hinder you on your fetch quest.  So what you will actually do is reveal a room, place the required number of clue tokens on it, then take investigate actions, with the associated skill check to try and collect those clue. And that’s it. Once you have enough clues, you spend them, read the next bit of story, then start the process all over again. 

The story telling system for this game is great. The agenda and act cards contribute to immerse yourself in the story, as does the campaign guide and the location flipping mechanism. However, the actual gameplay falls a bit flat for me. It kinds of remind me of Descent 2nd edition. Equip items and assets in different slots, then move around the board searching for clues and fighting.

So, it’s a strange game that doesn’t seem to know what it wants to be.

RATING: 8.5
I am a huge fan of [thing=77423][/thing] and it is one of my most played games.  I love deck construction and I love cooperative games.  While Lord of the Rings is one of my favorite games, I am neutral-positive about the Lovecraft theme, so I was very excited to try out [thing=205637][/thing].

You can see how much the designers have learned from 5 years of developing LotR.  Some of the clunky mechanics from LotR have been streamlined, and some of the best ideas have been highlighted.  

One of the main differences is that Arkham incorporates campaign play into its very essence.  This is mostly a positive because I like campaign play, but I also see this as somewhat of a negative because it is harder to just get together with friends and play a scenario.

The other main difference is that Arkham is more story-focused while LotR is more mechanics-focused.  As someone that has more of a mechanical mind, I think LotR will remain my favorite of the two.  However, I do enjoy the story.  My favorite aspect is that your campaign can continue even if you lose a scenario, and there are multiple resolutions to the scenarios instead of just win/lose.

Arkham Horror is exploring some exciting new design spaces that haven't been attempted before in a customizable card game, and I'm really looking forward to how it plays out.

RATING: N/A
1-4
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This was my most anticipated game in a long time. I've played every LCG (except, ironically, Call of Cthulhu). I love the Lord of the Rings LCG, which is similar to this game. So I had really high hopes for this one.

So far, it's great.

Controlling just one character (in LotR you control 2 or 3) as you make your way around Arkham, the depth of the scenarios, and having true campaign play are great. I've only played the first two scenarios from the core box (multiple times), but they show quite a bit of variety and they each have a whole different feel.

I was worried that this game would be too similar to LotR because of the similar mechanisms, but it really has a completely different feel. LotR has a really free form structure where you can play cards and take actions all throughout the round, but Arkham LCG feels more like a co-op board game where you take your turns with your three actions, then the game takes a turn, and repeat.

The economy of the game surprisingly feels more similar to Android: Netrunner. In both games you can spend actions to draw cards or get resources/credits, so you always feel like you're working up to something and trying to get the most out of every action.

There's been a lot of debate about one core set versus two, but you definitely need two core sets per player unless you just want to try out the game. The starter decks are really unreliable where you essentially have 30 unique cards, and getting the right pieces in play is completely up to the luck of the draw. With two core sets you put two copies of each important card, increasing the odds of drawing it and having a backup in case a game effect makes you lose it. Also, with two cores, two players can have two complete decks ready to go as long as they pick investigators of different factions (and sub-factions). 

The smaller decks and the limit of two copies per card mean that I'll be able to put more effort into deckbuilding with this game. As much as I love the other LCG's, I often just end up using decklists found online since I don't play enough to keep up with all of the new cards and deck archetypes. With smaller decks I can really go through all of the cards and see how everything interacts.

Update: I've now played through the entire campaign three times (twice with two players, once with three players) and it is fantastic. I've used all of the characters (except for Agnes) with decks that I've built, and I just love playing the game. So far the solo game is not so great, at least until we get more deckbuilding options. But with two and three players it's great.

I have to say they really nailed it with the first scenario. When you know what you're doing, you can beat it in 20-30 minutes so it serves as a great short intro to the game. More importantly, though, it's great for testing out new decks. Play a few rounds, make sure you can easily take care of the smaller enemies and have some way to deal with the larger ones, then you get to see how your deck can handle the boss. All in about 20 minutes. Great stuff.

[b]Player Count Verdict: 2-3[/b]
Here in the early life of the game, it's not very good solo. When the cardpool eventually increases then it should be more feasable, but for now the game is excellent with two and three players. I haven't played with four players yet, and while I'm sure it works well, it seems like downtime could start to be an issue.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Highly Thematic Card Co-op designed to be played in campaign mode.

Pros: Solid hand management. Really draws you into the theme. One of the few card games I've played with a "board" feeling. Solid campaign system with difficult impactful choices and a excellent level up system.

Cons: I really dislike the theme, to the point it ruins a lot of the experience for me. Winning/Losing is a bit fogged, as you progress to the campaign no matter what. It's more like live the experience and see the result. You need a lot of expansions to really see the game shine, the core set cards are really to few to show what this game can do.

This game does its best to polarise me. On the one end it's thematically terrible (for me) and I can't feel much agony since winning/losing are tonned down. On the other hand it's a mechanically engaging co-op game with a build in campaign system, which is exactly what I love! If this game had a different theme (say Star Wars, or Lord of the Rings) and a more clear impact of the individual missions it would be my all star favourite game. As it is now, I just like to play it and that's it.

RATING: 8
As I expected, I enjoyed [thing=205637]Arkham TCG[/thing]. But is difficult to give opinions regarding it at this point: I don't know all the scenarios; I don't know the cards (I didn't built, nor want to, the decks); I don't even see all the investigators. It is so many holes that is hard to properly give a view of the game.

Yet, what I can say about it, is that it has two great mechanics: the chaos bag, that work similar as a dice to randomize bonus or penalties to tests, but it allows for customization, making possible to increase or decrease the challenge, simply by changing the distribution of tokens in the bag. The other is the way locations work in order to form the "board", by way of connections the investigators do move in a proper manner between places. It is very well thought, thematic and highly functional, specially for a card game, and it can also be customized to form new scenarios.

Even without seeing the whole game, I can already tell that the system is well done, both for the simplicity of the card play as well as for the possibilities, going from building the decks, to using icons on cards in order to help in tests, which makes cards being helpful in more ways - many times, in LotR CG I had cards that I simply couldn't use, for lack of resources or that they wouldn't have a use in the situation; this will occur much less often in [thing=205637]Arkham TCG[/thing]. Also, simply by having a proper campaign mode, it has a major plus with me. Finally, the Mythos theme is deeply present and well applied.

These are the strong points that I saw. The weak spots are the same old ones (not to be confused with the Great Old Ones): the LCG model; and the constant building and tweaking of decks - there are those that enjoy this, following the meta, learning and discussing what goes with what; but I'm not one of them - I just like to sit and play.

Overall, since I don't own (and, thus, don't have to keep buying expansions) nor build the decks, [thing=205637]Arkham TCG[/thing] only have positives for me so far. Easily recommended.

RATING: 5
Only played the main core scenarios once:
The story telling and the resulting differences depending on how you worked out in earlier scenarios is really great and fun.

But not the token drawing thing in this game. Seems FFG wanted a new cool way for the overused skill checks with dice / 5+ rolls. Epic Fail in my opinion... or maybe just in this game.
On Normal you need to "overpower" your skill test with +2 to get a 50/50 chance to succeed. By many tests you need to boost with cards and resources, after mid game you have only one try left with a boost, then all cards / resources are gone (again).
Unfair is not difficult, my opinion.
To enjoy the game you have to play on easy, which is too easy, almost boring...
There are 4 different difficulties to set up the tokens, but none of theme is a good mix of competition and fun.

The deck building is way too less for my taste, after each scenario spending 10, 15min, separating your deck to just get 3 or 4 new / better cards seems not worth the time.

Only reason for the hype: Theme!

Recommendation: Please try the campaign for the main game, before you buy it, like my rating says "take it or leave it".

RATING: 7.5
This was my introduction to living card games. I'm fairly familiar with Lovecraftian lore and the various Fantasy Flight releases revolving around Arkham Horror, so the content was nothing new to me. The AHLCG takes an established property and breaths some new life into it. All the familiar hallmarks of Arkham Horror are there, including the dreaded mythos phase, ability checks, and clue tokens. Refreshingly, FFG have decided to drop any dice play from the game, and instead, checks are resolved by drawing tokens out of a "chaos bag". These tokens feature numbers ranging from -7 onto +2, which indicate whether or not your character has been successful. The LCG gives players more control over the checks by allowing certain cards to be played prior, which can "boost" the result of the check. To add a minor variable of chance, there are still instant fail and instant success tokens thrown in.

The major complaint I've heard regarding the base set of the LCG is that it's rather short at only three scenarios. While I'd agree with this sentiment, I'd say that the core set serves as a useful demo for players who are unsure if they want to take the plunge deeper into the depths of the AHLCG.

RATING: 5

I do have to give the game credit for its narrative and immersive world, but as a game, Arkham Horror LCG is simply not for me.

RATING: 2
TL;DR:
Low value for the core box
Little player agency, a lack of meaningful choices
Scenario Narrative is unsatisfying
Lots of art is reused from other games
Lots of cards in the base set are built to be ineffective

The game in this box supports only 2 players, and thats if they choose exclusive deck builds. The game assumes every player will buy at least 1 core set, and each player would need 2 core sets in order to have a collection that could play characters that use overlapping deck builds.

The times I played through there was little agency. The scenario progressed in a linear fashion and options were limited at each point. At the end of the scenario, we learned that 2 of the characters cannot win the first scenario with any of the cards available to them in the base game.

I was surprised at the lack of approaches, since Elder Sign, Arkham Horror and Eldritch Horror not only allow multiple approaches but require them in order to overcome obstacles.

RATING: N/A
I'm inclined to give this a decent rating, but I'm not sure that we played correctly.  Learning a new game at 10 pm is never a good idea, particularly when you're nose-deep in the rulebook for long stretches.

I will say that there's an ocean (ok, at least a sea) of downtime with four players.  One factor was the chit-drawing mechanic, which could have easily been replaced by a specially designed d10 or d12.  To speed things up, you could opt for simultaneous turns - and we did towards the end - but multi-horror solitaire isn't my style.  I'd try again with two players, assuming that my fellow investigator (i.e., the game's owner) learns the rules intimately.  Or at least gets to second base.

Also, the boss battle at the end is a grind.  That was unexpected, and not necessarily in a good way.

RATING: 7
FFG's two-rulebook system failed me on this one.  I've never played a LCG before, and found a lot of stuff during our first play that I just couldn't find answers to in either rulebook.  Usually it's in one or the other, but we really had a hard time with this one.  Component-wise, it's standard FFG quality, which is good.  The art is nice. Gameplay is ok for now, probably would be better with fewer stoppages to look up rules. The core set contains 3 scenarios that make up one campaign and further scenarios/campaigns are to come in the extra packs and expansions ... so it'll be expensive to keep playing. As for replayability, it's more like re-playing a boss fight in a video game than it is re-reading the same chapter of a book for the 12th time. That is to say, it should be fine. Obviously more scenarios will help, but you could probably play through the core campaign a few times without getting tired of it.

RATING: 7
This one is a complicated one for me. I have hard mixed feelings about it.

It fluctuates between: it's a involving game that portraits Lovecraft's world in a pretty cool. To: I cannot deal with an LGC and I was expecting something else.

I played easy mode. Started the campaign with 2 players. But after the first game I had to continue alone, since I couldn't get my girlfriend to go back to it and being punched in the face until we were dead.

So first, this is a hard game, HARD. Which, sure, being a co-op, it has to be hard, bla bla, ya. But still, I don't believe that I had any change (specially on that 3rd scenario). What makes really sad and weary is that I truly believe that FFG don't expect you to win it with just one copy of the base game. Since the cards available are not good enough to build a proper deck(s). And to that, I say: SCREW YOU! It just feels unfair. Give me, in the "base game", enough for me to have a "base ability" to fight back, or at least to stand a change.

On another hand (and here it might be my TIME Stories' previous-story-based-game-experience-and-expectations), I was expecting something else. I do think the mechanics are good, but I was expecting more of a puzzle (since we're playing detectives) and less of a pure skill checker game. There's a lot of decisions to take on this game, sure. But I felt that none actually matter (with the base game only) in face to enemies. Having, only, as weapons my(our) crappy decks and that devilish bag of tokens.
I have to give it, after the 2nd scenario I started to see the true potential of the system. It did get me curious with it for the future.

In the end, maybe, just maybe, in some years, if I find someone selling a big collection (which includes way more than the base game for sure) I might buy it again and have a proper full experience. For now, I'm trading it away. 

Traded.

RATING: 3
I am guessing that Fantasy Flight went with Arkham Horror: the Card Game because Arkham Horror: The Chit Bag Game was less catchy even though its more accurate. I mean sure there are cards in the game and you get to play them, but your going to spend more time pulling chits out of a bag than playing cards, and the cards you do play only facilitate pulling chits out of a bag better. 
Speaking of Chit bags, this particular chit bag is the opposite of fun and is functionally the same as rolling a really shitty die. One that you have to roll for everything you want to do. If you want to add difficulty to the game, your out of luck, because the difficulty levels are just making the already shit die even MORE shit YAY, let chance rolls take care of difficulty instead of making the scenario longer. But that would involve including more cards in a card game, which is no good apparently.
OH speaking of more cards, There are not enough cards in this game for deck customization, Not a good sign for a game that markets itself as a "deck building game." I would like to believe that each character plays very different and is very unique but when every character shares 10 or more cards with each other I just don't see it. Not that I'm going to play again to find out.

RATING: 10
Just about what I want and expect out of an Arkham Files game. The game places you on the shoes of an investigator and it works pretty well. Very immersive.

I read complaints about deckbuilding options right out of the box being rather limited, which is true (unless you buy two boxes, which I did), but to me, unlike other deckbuilders, regular actions like moving around from locations, exploring and investigating and fighting/evading monsters is what this is all about, to a point where the card game aspect becomes secondary to the game (so much so that you could probably play with the suggested decks and do "ok", even if they're pretty bland), and I think this is just fine.
